THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: THIRD PERIOD)
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 123 of 1981)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Apple and Pear Export Charge Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 147 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Export Ch~rge Act 1976.
Apple and Pear Levy Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 1460f 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Levy Act 1976.
Appropriation Act (No.1) 1981-82 (Act No. 1640f 1981)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1982.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1981-82 (Act No. 165 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year endingon 30June 1981.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 145 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 124 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 126 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 125 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 130 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (lnsurance Bus;ness) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Life Insurance Business) Act 1981 (Act No. 128 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of certain life insurance business having a connection with the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981 (Act No. 129 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of the registration of certain vehicles in the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian CapitalTerritory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 127 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 150 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 50 of the Austrillian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Act 1977.
Australian National University Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 1660f 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Natiollal University Act 1946.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1981 (Act No. 168 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964, and for related purposes.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 177 of 1981)An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905, and for related purposes.
Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 151 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Act 1905 in relation to the exportation of food.
Commonwealth Grants Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 173 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Grants Commission Act 1973.
Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 153 of 1981)- An Act to amend laws relating to companies and securities and for related purposes.
Continental Shelf (Living Natural Resources) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 182 of 1981)An Act to amend the Continental Shelf(Living Natural Resources) Act 1968 to facilitate the collection of levy imposed by the Foreign Fishing Boats Levy Act 1981 and of amounts payable under certain agreements, and for certain other purposes.
Crimes (Currency) Aat 1981 (Act No. 1220f 1981)An Act to make provision with respect to offences connected with the counterfeiting of money or certain kinds of securities and other activities injurious to Australian currency, and for related purposes.
Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 152 of 1981)- An Act ~to amend the Cllstoms Act 1901 in relation to the exportation of food.

Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 1.45 p.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Community Youth Support Scheme
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled.
The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia who respectfully showeth their anger and condemnation at the Fraser Government's decision to abolish all Community Youth Support Schemes on 31 st October 1981.
Further they denounce the decision for the disastrous effects it will have on growing numbers of unemployed young people around Australia.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Federal Government immediately and fully reverse its decision and restore complete funding to the Community Youth Support Scheme.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Lionel Bowen, Mr Coleman, Mr Cowan, Mr Kerin, Mr Les McMahon and Mr O'Keefe.

Community Youth Support Scheme
To the honourable, the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully demands:
That the decision of the Government to end funding for the Community Youth Support Schemes on October 31st, 1981 as announced by the Treasurer in the Budget speech of 18th August, 1981 be reversed.
Community Youth Support Schemes have operated successfully for five years and provide community support and educational opportunities for unemployed young people which will not be adequately provided by other Government programmes. The full effect of the abolition of Community Youth Support Schemes will be felt by the unemployed people whose opportunities to participate in the Australian Community will be further diminished.
The petitioners humbly pray that the House of Representatives take all steps necessary to ensure that continued Government funding of Community Youth Support Schemes.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Wilson.
Petition received.
Community Youth Support Scheme
To the honourable, the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Victoria respectfully showeth:
That the serious youth unemployment problem is a direct result of the Community's failure to create sufficient jobs.
In these circumstances the decision to abolish Community Youth Support Schemes (CYSS) is a socially retrogressive step. The CYSS projects provide valuable support services and work training for many young unemployed.
We the undersigned are concerned that the closure of CYSS projects may lead young unemployed to become involved in anti-social behaviour such as crime, vandalism, prostitution, rebellious behaviour and mob action. There is the even more frightening prospect that many more young people will give up hope of obtaining a job, become idle and even contemplate suicide.

Unemployment
To the Honourable Speaker and Members bf the House of Representatives of the Parliament of the Commonwealth of Australia, in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia, respectfully showeth that:
While an average of over 450,000 Australians are now suffering unemployment at anyone time,
over one million people have been unemployed for one or more weeks during the last year and of these,
62% of women unemployed and 41 % of men unemployed received no unemployment benefits, and further that While the poverty line for a family (one income, one child) is $117.90 per week and the average weekly earnings are $243 per week, the income for these one million unemployed Australians is on average $62 per week for women and $110 per week for men.
We the undersigned citizens therefore declare
that unemployment with its attendant consequences of massive poverty is the major domestic, political and human question facing the National Government at this time,
that the central issue for the'! 980 Federal elections must be the economy and the creation of jobs so that all Australians who choose may work.
We call for the right to work guaranteed as a fundamental human
right the restoration of full employment the maintenance of real wages and the creation of accept-
able social conditions the maintenance and expansion of the public sector

Privacy and Personal Information
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of ,Australia respectfully showeth that:
Paragraphs 107 and 108 of the Privacy and Personal Information discussion paper number 14, brought down by the Law Reform Commission in June 1980, undermines the family unit and the authority of parents, which is the Christian basis of our society, by proposing that "between 12 and 16 years, a child should be entitled to object to disclosure of information to a parent".
It is your petitioners' sincere belief that:
(a) an adolescent between the ages of 12 and 16 is not at a mature enough stage of liFe to decide whether or not the disclosure of information to a parent will be in the best interests of the adolescent.
(b) as a record keeper has no responsibility For the child, and as his moral standards may be different to those of a parent, he should not be permitted to come between child and parent.
(c) a parent has a unique position whicl1 may enable him/her to perceive a situation that the record keeper does not. The parent must therefore be aware of the problem if remedial action is to be taken, and has the right to guide the child in the direction he/she feels fit.
Your petitioners thereFore pray:
That the Members of the House of Representatives will reject the recommendation of the Law Reform Commission and find some other means to protect children from the excessive abuse of some parents.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Dobie.
Petition received.

Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker, and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble Petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
I. That the decisions arising out of the Review of Government Functions (undertaken by The Razor Gang) in relation to tertiary education funding will severely impair the process of developing the nation's intellectual resources.
2. That the inevitable outcome of the decisions will, in general terms be~
(i) the financial impoverishment of small and mediumsized universities;
(ii) the abandonment of non-vocational courses and the possibility of widespread academic unemployment.
3. That amongst the particular consequences of the decisions, namely consequences following on the re-introduction of tertiary fees for selected categories of post-graduate students will be-
(i) the discouraging of part-time post-graduate students and all those not receiving Commonwealth or University scholarships;
(ii) the weakening of the national research effort to which post-graduate students have in the past made a significant contribution.
4. That the provision of the maximum TEAS benefit to only 14% of all students, indeed, the failure to adequately support financially all undergraduate students, will be a disincentive to enrolment in tertiary institutions.
Your Petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House will request the Government to re-consider the decisions arising out of the Review of Commonwealth Functions and urgently initiate a full and complete inquiry into the following:
(i) employment in tertiary institutions; (ii) the effects of the Government's decisions upon the
national research effort and upon post-graduate enrolments; (iii) the adequacy of tertiary student support systems.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys.

To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. This Petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
(i) the financial situation of students enrolled at Australian post-secondary institutions is already extremely difficult (as indicated in the Commonwealth Department of Education's 1979 survey of student incomes and expenditure) and any substantial increase in the financial commitments of students would gravely worsen their financial difficulties.
(ii) the imposition of post-secondary tuition fees of comparable levels to those imposed prior to 1971 would force many currently enrolled students to discontinue their studies and deter many persons willing and able at present to undertake post-secondary study from doing so.
(iii) those students and potential students from social groups currently under-represented and disadvantaged in the post-secondary education system would be most severely affected by the reintroduction of fees for post-secondary education.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
I. post-secondary education tuition fees not be reintroduced for any courses or for any category of students
2. the visa charges upon private overseas students be removed immediately ,

3. 'the Minister of Education immediately clarify the Government's attitude to the reintroduction of postsecondary tuition fees.

And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Rocher.

Staging of American B52 Bombers
To the Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned in the Northern Territory respectfully showeth:
That the proposed use of Darwin Airport as a refuelling and Rest and Recreation base for American B52 bombers:
Jeopardises the welfare of the citizens of Darwin and of all Australians, in that it establishes a stronger potential of Darwin being a nuclear target in any conflict between the USA and another power.
Impedes the sovereignty of the Australian nation in that Australian citizens would have neither control nor knowledge of whether B52's:
(a) Carried nuclear weapons (b) Were engaged in bombing missions against another
State.
Violates the Australian people's independence and links this nation irreconcilably with United States foreign policies thereby destroying the potential for independent Australian defence policy.
Violates democratic procedures in committing the Australian nation to participation in future wars without due consultation and -informed assessment by Australian citizens and further increases the militarisation of the Indian Ocean thereby increasing the likelihood of super power conflict in this region.
Jeopardises the welfare of the Citizens of Darwin, in that noise pollution and the threat of accidental radioactive fallout will increase substantially to the residents.
Negates the potential of the Australian nation to establish itself as a nation of Peace in the Pacific region and violates Australia's commitment to the Nuclear NonProliFeration Treaty.
Your petitioners therefore pray that the Members of the House of Representatives will ensure that the Prime Minister withdraw the offer of Darwin, or any other location in Australia, as a base or depot for American B52 bombers. And that furthermore will ensure that any request of such usage by the US Government be rejected.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrKent.

ECONOMY
Sir WILLIAM McMAHON-My question, which is directed to the Prime Minister, is in effect a request to review the assumptions on which the Budget was based. Is the Prime Minister aware of the adverse economic and financial trends in the economy that commenced at the beginning of 1981? Does he agree that the assumption of a very large flow of private capital into Australia, which was claimed to be an essential ingredient of success, has not occurred? Does he know that there has been a fall in our international reserves from $6.1 billion to $4.45 billion in August, with a consequent serious effect on the money supply? Does the Prime Minister know that the Reserve Bank of Australia has somewhat tardily increased lending by the trading banks by2 per cent over the previous level? Does this indicate that the Budget estimate was wrong because the minimum liquidity ratio for trading banks went down to the minimum of about 18 per cent? As well, does the Prime Minister agree that the barbarised interpretation of the Gregory thesis by government officials-that industries, mainly manufacturing, had to move over or go broke in order to provide space for energy-related industries-has proved to be wrong? Would he inform manufacturers that tariff changes will be carefully chosen over a reasonable period? Has the Gregory argument been proved wrong by the August trade figures, which showed that imports were $1,834m and exports were $1 ,685m, and by the growing unused industrial capacity of this country? Will he look at the changes in the exchange rate, and the enormous and debilitating losses to Australian producers who export, because of the revaluation of the currency, which is still going on, and the losses which have been caused to Australian manufacturers competing with imports, with the consequent effect on unemployment and gross nonfarm production? I ask these questions because of the Prime Minister's very deep knowledge. I ask that a committee be established, comprising the Prime Minister, the Deputy Prime Minister, the Minister for Industry and Commerce, the Treasurer and the Minister for Primary Industry, so that these matters can be considered.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-Mr Speaker--
Mr Dawkins-Did you get notice of that?
Mr Barry Jones-He gave it to him.
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member for Lalor will remain silent. I am quite sure that the Prime Minister does not need the

honourable member's help in answering the question.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-Oh, I am grateful for it. The Government regularly reviews the
economy and what is happening in the economy. The Government regularly gets reports from the Treasurer on changes in the economy. That, of course, will proceed as a matter of course. But I think there are some aspects other than those to which the right honourable gentleman drew
attention which also ought to be borne in mind. In recent times there have been some encouraging bond sales. That has been a good turnaround from earlier weeks when, at the prevailing rates, it had been difficult to sell Commonwealth paper. Capital inflow certainly has been much less over the last several weeks than it was throughout the generality of last year. The capital inflow is notoriously volatile and, obviously, would be influenced by the very high interest rates in the United States. Despite rates being high in this country, they are still not as high as they are in the United States. That, I think, is significantly to the credit of the broad thrust of the Government's economic policies. Obviously, again, a very significant domestic Budget surplus would be a significant element in that.
Lack of capital inflow, as is the case in so many economic matters, has some good points and some bad points about it. From one point, it makes it much easier to achieve the Government's monetary targets. The fact that the trading banks have been given permission to extend their advances at a slightly greater rate is obviously recognition by the Reserve Bank of Australia that some accommodation in that area is helpful. I do not think anyone should read too much into that change, which has been announced today but which took effect some weeks ago, because, quite plainly, the Reserve Bank, through the banking system, will be making adjustments from time to time. The settings are not made at the time of the Budget and left there inflexibly throughout the year. One would expect the Reserve Bank, through the banking system, to be monitoring and watching what is happening in the economy. The Reserve Bank has reacted in a way which I think many people would regard as responsible.
The right honourable g~ntleman also raised the question of tariffs. I think that in everything the Government has done we have indicated that we wanted to moderate the impact of high protection but, at the same time, do it in a reasonable and gradual manner which will not unduly disrupt Australian industry but which, at the same time, will provide an incentive to greater efficiency and rationalisation within Australian industry. What

Questions Without Notice

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1377

we did in relation to the textiles, apparel and footwear industry a little over 12 months ago, I think, is a significant example of that. The decision not only gave confidence to the industry but also established the circumstances in which a larger part of the domestic market would be available for import competition as each year passed. In relation to tariffs and protection matters, that is the way in which the Government wants to operate. It wants to operate in a way which maintains the confidence of domestic industry but which, at the same time, encourages greater efficiency.
Revaluation has obviously reduced returns on Australian exports. But, again, the revaluation against the basket of currencies needs to be looked at against the background of the movements in relation to the Australian dollar and the United States dollar. The movements have gone in the opposite' direction and therefore have greatly moderated the impact on many Australian exporters. This has taken place over a significant period. Nevertheless, a strong Australian dollar is something which I think, by and large, all Australians want. There is not the slightest doubt that the appreciation of the Australian dollar over the last 12 months is one of the factors which led to the moderate inflation rate over the last 12 months. If we had had a weak dollar there would have been much greater impetus to inflation from overseas, with unhappy results for Australian producers and exporters. In making those comments '1 have sought to put the position contained in the right honourable gentleman's question into perspective. Nevertheless, the points he raised have been noted by the Treasurer and by me and his point of view certainly will be taken into account when we are examining these matters.

WELFARE HOUSING
Mr HICKS'----I ask the Minister for Housing and Construction whether welfare housing is a responsibility shared by the Commonwealth and State governments. If so, are the States shouldering their responsibilities?
Mr McVEIGH-I am grateful to the honourable member for Riverina for an excellent question. It is true that since 1945-the iirst year of the Commonwealth-State Housing Agreement on

welfare housing-there has been a shared responsibility between the States and the Commonwealth. At this stage only two of the States have released their Budgets, so one cannot answer the honourable member's question generally. However, it seems to me to be appropriate to compare the two State Budgets that have been announced. I take South Australia first. We find that in its Budget which was announced yesterday that State saw fit to increase its allocation for welfare housing by the huge sum of $17m, or by 30 per cent.
Mr Braithwaite-How much?
Mr McVEIGH-An increase of 30 per cent or $17m. Quite obviously, the Minister of Housing in South Australia took note of my advice to him at a .recent meeting in Sydney when I suggested to him that he should obtain more money from his State. The action of the South Australian Government reflects its attitude over the years. We find that in 1979-80, the last year for which statistics are available, that State Government spent $47 a head on welfare housing.
I turn to the other State which has announced its Budget. The question sought a comparison of those States that are sharing and those that are not sharing the housing welfare burden. We find that in New South Wales the allocation for housing decreased by 16 per cent and the allocation for welfare housing decreased by 14 per cent. Those decreases reflect that Government's concern for welfare housing over the years. In the year in which I said South Australia was giving $47 a head, New South Wales gave the second lowest allocation in the Commonwealth. It gave only $13' a head.
Mr Beazley-How much is it in Victoria?
Mr McVEIGH-It is $6 in Victoria. It concerns me to find that New South Wales has the highest number of people on the waiting list for welfare housing in Australia-38,000. I know that I am not supposed to advertise-I take note of that-but this morning in the Sydney Morning Herald, which is supposed to be a good newspaper, it was pointed out that in New South Wales at least 66 per c~nt of the people in welfare housing should not be t\1ere because their incomes are above the permissiole level. In answer to the excellent question asked by the honourable member for Riverina I come to this conclusion: New South Wales just is not interested in providing funds for welfare housing; it would rather spend the taxpayers' money on funding frivolous election campaigning.

COMMONWEALTH HEADS OF GOVERNMENT MEETING

BANK LENDING
Mr SHACK-My question is directed to the Treasurer and I too refer to the announcement last night by the Reserve Bank of Australia that there would be a relaxation of trading bank lending constraints. If it is true, as has been reported, that several of the banks have been lending over the previous level, what likely real effect, if any, will this relaxation have on satisfying the high demand for funds and on reducing interest rate pressures?
Mr HOWARD-The first thing that should be said about the announcement last night by the Reserve Bank is that it was very much an in-thegroove announcement by a central bank.
Mr Hayden-John Sidney will send you another telegram if you talk like that.
Mr HOWARD-For people to start reading it as being anything more than an appropriate adjustment of quantitative gUIdance by the Reserve Bank to the trading banks in Australia is to completely misread the situation. To imply this, as the Leader of the Opposition has done-his interjection reminds me that it will result in another $900m of bank lending being available in the

community-is to misunderstand the situation totally. As the Prime Minister pointed out in answer to an earlier question, it is necessary that none of the settings against which economic decisions are made at the beginning of the year should be regarded as immutable and unchangeable. Equally, I do not think we should panic, take fright, or over-react to two months' figures.
A lot of nonsense has been talked about the figures for private capital inflow into Australia over the last couple of months. I take the opportunity of telling the House that the private capital inflow into Australia for the months of July and August 1981 was 50 per cent greater than for the months of July and August 1980; in other words, $805m versus $545m. We are dealing with a situation where, during those two months, far from there being a deterioration in the volume of private capital inflow into Australia there has been a widening of the current account deficit because of a much greater volume of imports and a lesser volume of exports.
In part, some of those reasons are of a one-off character. For example, in both July and August the import figures were inflated by the importation of a number of Boeing aircraft by one of Australia's domestic airlines, which made a very significant contribution towards bolstering the import figures during those months. Equally, the disastrous strike in central Queensland has had a dramatic effect on coal exports from that State, and there have been other industrial problems. What I put to the House-it is a little away from the honourable gentleman's question, I am prepared to concede, but I think the two matters are related-is that it has been argued that in some way what is nothing more than a technical adjustment to the lending guidance given by the Reserve Bank to our trading banks has in some way been a panic reaction to greatly adverse balance of payments figures.
Private capital inflow is a notoriously volatile item. We made the best estimate at the time the Budget was brought down. For the first two months of this financial year it is 50 per cent better than it was last year. Nobody can swear to this House that it will be precisely what was predicted at Budget time but for anybody to suggest, as the Leader of the Opposition has done, that in some way because of these figures the Budget strategy is .collapsing demonstrates a total misreading of the situation.
STARLAB AND THE SYNTHESIS TELESCOPE
Mr BARRY JONES-I direct my question to the Prime Minister. Is it a fact that Australia is

1380 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Questions Without Notice

considered to be one of the leading nations in the world in radio astronomy? Is it also a fact that the Government has declined to provide any money whatever in the 1981-82 Budget for two vital proj-
ects in radio astronomy-the synthesis telescope at Parkes and the Starlab project, a. joint enterprise of the United States, Canada and Australia? Does the Government realise the enormous economic potential that Starlab will have if it leads, as expected, to the production of new crystalline structures in metal? Does the Government recognise the damage that our refusal to provide any money will do to our international reputation in this important area? Finally, will the Prime Minister reconsider the failure to provide any money for these two projects in the
1981-82 Budget?
Mr THOMSON-The honourable member for Lalor is correct. The Government did not provide any money for the synthesis telescope in this year's Budget. It was a Budget of restraint and the honourable member is well aware of the reasons for that restraint. A submission was made for an expenditure of $140,000 on a feasibility study of Starlab. That funding was not included in the Budget. I am very happy to say that the Australian National University and a French firm, Matra, have come to an agreement to carry out a feasibility study. The Government will look with great interest at the results of that study.

both at ministerial and departmental level. Interdepartmental consultations are well under way and I anticipate-I do not comment with any sense of guarantee to this House-that the Cabinet will be in a position to commence discussions on the report some time towardsthe end of October.
I also anticipate, as the House would well understand, that because of the importance of tQe issue and its complexity there will be several Cabinet meetings before we are in a position to take a final decision. There can be no denying that this will be a very difficult decision for the Government to take. The industry itself is divided and there is very considerable and significant interest by various sections within the Australian community. A number of major interest groups are well publicising their views at this time. I emphasise that the Government is not open to emotional pressures from any particular interest group, whether in support of or opposed to the present shape of the industry and the level of assistance that it receives. Having said that, the Government, of course, is open to sound argument backed by good evidence from whatever source. It is on this and on the Government's assessment of Australia's national interests, having regard to the needs of the industry, that we will take our decision on the level of assistance which should apply to the industry.

Those discussions have taken place

SPECIAL TRANSPORT NEEDS GRANTS
Mr MORRIS-My question is directed to the Minister for Transport. I note that he is satisfied that the Victorian Government is making a very valuable contribution to the road programs in that State. So I ask': Why, in these circumstances, has the Government made a special transport needs grant of $70m to Victoria? Will the Minister state the nature of the special transport needs which are evident in that State, apart from the approaching defeat of the Victorian Government? Will he also itemise the specific transport projects on which the $70m is to be expended? Finally, will the Minister now act to provide similar special transport needs grants to Queensland, Tasmania, Western A\lstralia, South Australia, the Northern Territory and in particular New South Wales as that State has increased its road expenditure by 28 per cent in the last four years?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-This matter arose directly out of the last Premiers Conference. I think it needs to be understood that nearly every State had had one and maybe more than one special addition to the semi-government loans programs over the years. For example, New South Wales had had special additions totalling

Questions Without Notice

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1381

$IOOm for the special transport needs-railways needs-of that State. It received $1 OOm or $20m a
year. That amount was provided after the train
disaster in New South Wales to enable that State
to catch up on a lag in the railways and in railway
quality because insufficient funds had been provided for those purposes in earlier years. Victoria
was one State which had never had special ad-
ditions to semi-government loan programs over all the years. Therefore, there was a commitment
of $20m this year for the semi-government
program and $20m next year, making a total of $40m in all. In addition, there was a grant of $35m
this year and next year off the Commonwealth
Budget. This money was provided in general
terms to meet some transport disabilities of the State and of the city of Melbourne. But the funds
were to be used, quite obviously, as was the case with New South Wales, at the discretion of the
State Government.

So far as the real impact of all this is concerned,
what matters on the domestic monetary aggregates, of course, is the surplus of private sector
foreign exchange transactions. As I pointed out in answer to the honourable member for Tangney,
there has been a widening of the current account
deficit because imports have grown at a much fas-
ter rate than exports over the past few months. I
touched briefly upon the reasons in my answer to the honourable member for Tangney. But all of this underlines the need for the Government and
for the Reserve Bank of Australia, given its par-
ticular responsibilities for the conduct of the mon-
etary system, to maintain a flexible attitude and to
be willing to change things such as the quantitative guidance to the Australian trading banks, and
it also puts some kind of obligation on us and
those who follow us not to overreact to what are
proper technical adjustments by the central banking authorities.

Statistics Catalogue which states:

of the report which states:

million.

Aborigines have become the invisible, unseen and unheard people of Australia. They neither share in the prosperity nor the promise of a free, democratic and affluent nation. It is in
the interests of all the racially oppressed that the plight of the Aboriginal people be known overseas.

I point out that taking into account that $90m, it brings the figure down to $270m. How does the Treasurer reconcile that with what he has just said to the House remembering that I have spoken about the nine months from the beginning of 1981? This refers only to the capital inflow.
Mr HOWARD-With great respect to my right honourable friend, I think it is relatively easy to reconcile that with what I have said to the House. I said a number of things to the House. I said firstly that one ought not to read too much into a month or two's figures, whether those figures are favourable or unfavourable. I do not propose to start panicking over the fact that in July and August of this year private capital inflow has not been as strong as it was in December of last year when it was $27Im. But I repeat to the right honourable gentIemanthat in July of last year private capital inflow was $268m. In August it was $277m. In July of this year it was $426m. In August of this year it was $379m.

In view of the Prime Minister's persistent preaching against the racist policies of other countries, when will his Government implement policies supported by proper levels of finance to do away with the appalling conditions of the Aboriginal people described in this report? MINISTERIAL ARRANGEMENTS
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Leader of the House)-On behalf of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) I inform the House that the. Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar) left Australia earlier today to attend a regional meeting of the World Health Organisation in Korea and to participate in discussions with medical officials in China, Japan, Hong Kong and the Philippines. In his absence, the Minister for Aboriginal Affairs (Senator Peter Baume) is acting as Minister for Health. The Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) will represent the Minister for Health in this chamber until Mr MacKellar returns to Australia on 5 October.

INDUSTRIAL RELATIONS INQUIRY

Mr SPEAKER-The Minister for Industrial Relations has indicated to me that he wishes my indulgence on a matter,

Mr VINER (Stirling-Minister for Industrial Relations)-Mr Speaker, yesterday the honourable member for Wills (Mr Hawke) asked me for some information regarding consultations with the States on the national inquiry into wage determination and industrial relations. I have provided him with that information by letter today. I seek leave to incorporate the letter in Hansard. I have spoken to the honourable member and he has agreed to its incorporation.

Leave granted.

The document read as follows-

Dear Mr Hawke,

I refer to the Question without Notice you asked me yesterday concerning steps I had taken to have consultations with the States on the National Inquiry into Wage Determination
and Industrial Relations.

Formal consultations have been held with the Slates at the
Premiers' Conferences on 4 May, 20 June and 13 Augusl, 1981.

Further formal consultation look place al the Special Meet-
ing of the Conference of Commonwealth and State Labour Ministers on 5 August, 1981.

Revised Terms of Reference will be discussed al the forthcoming Conference of the Commonwealth and Slate
Labour Ministers in Darwin thi~ Friday.

There has also been correspondence belween myself and all of the State Labour Ministers as well as discussions bet ween the Department of Industrial Relations and Slate Depart-
ments at the official level.

Mr R. Hawke, A.a., M.P., Member for Wills, Parliament House, CANBERRA, ACT 2600

Yours sincerely, R.l. VINER

PRIVATE CAPITAL INFLOW
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr SPEAKER-I have received a letter from the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The serious implications of the Government's increasing reliance on large scale private capital inflow.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number ofmembln required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (3.16)- Throughout most of the post-war era Australia has relied on private capital inflow to supplement its domestic capital and to balance its balance of payments. That development has had serious implications for the level of foreign ownership and control of our industry and resources which has inexorably increased throughout this period. But recently we entered a quite new phase, one in which the level of private capital inflow has increased vastly and which carries with it not only intensified foreign ownership and control implications but also considerable implications for budgetary and monetary policy and for exchange rate policy. As these macro-economic levers are adjusted to accommodate the new high levels of capital inflow, considerable structural changes are being foisted on our economy. Because of the highly volatile nature of capital inflow, a much greater degree of imprecision, uncertainty and variability pertains to economic policy than would otherwise be the case.
Recently we have seen a dramatic increase in private capital inflow. Prior to last financial year the level was never over $2,000m. Last year, however, it increased to a staggering $6.5 billion. This year the Budget estimate is for it to be even higher. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) said the day after the Budget, in answer to a question in the House, that the Budget was framed on the expected inflow of capital of $6 billion to $7 billion. However, last week, in answer to a question at the Estimates committee hearings for the Department of the Tteasury, he agreed that the Budget estimate for capital inflow is $7.8 billion. This is a stupendous amount-another $1.3 billion over what was actually incurred last year

1388 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Private Capital Inflow

and four times the level of two years ago, which figure was itself the highest level ever in our history.
Why has this enormous increase occurred? Obviously iUs related, at least in part, to the upsurge in resource development. But the extent to which this is so is not at all clear. The fact is that the Government does not know what a lot of this
capital inflow is for. There are simply no ngures available to tell us exactly what it is being used for. The day after the Budget the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) said that he had been advised by the Reserve Bank of Australia that the inflow was related substantially to resource development but that the Bank was not exactly' .sure to what degree and that he had asked the bank to get him more information. The Treasurer has also
said since then, presumably responding to rising public concern, that he has asked the Reserve Bank and the Treasury to find out where foreign investment in Australia is actually going.
What an incredible situation this is. We let the foreign investment in first and then we try to find out what it was for and where it all went. One would have thought that its intended use would have been a relevant consideration in deciding whether to allow it here in the first place. It is also relevant in this regard to recall that this Government has abolished that section of the Australian Bureau of Statistics which monitored the degree of foreign ownership of Australian industries, so we do not know any more what extent foreign ownership and control of our various industries has reached. Thus, this Government has taken a massive leap in the dark. It has encouraged an enormous increase in private capital inflow without knowing where it is going, what it it being used for, to what degree it is being used for speculative activity rather than productive use and to what extent it is resulting in loss of Australian ownership and control of our various industries.
This also has significant budgetary and monetary implications. Capital inflow, particularly large scale capital inflow, clearly affects our budgetary and monetary strategy because to the extent that it exceeds the private current account deficit on the balance of payments it adds to the money supply. Last year the effect of capital inflow on the money supply was enormous. It accounted for $2.8 billion of our growth in M3, which was a 45 per cent growth in that measure of the money supply. That of course is an extraordinarily high proportion, far higher than any previous year in our history. This year the Budget estimate is that it will add the same money amount as last year, that is $2.8 billion, to the money supply and because of expected budgeted lower growth in the

money supply it will add about the same percentage to the money supply as last year, that is, a little under half the total growth in the money supply.
Because of this large impact on the money
supply from this private capital inflow the Government then has to take action to offset that impact on the money supply. Last year the Government reduced the Budget domestic deficit by around $1 billiona,nd turned it into a small surplus and so took money out of the economy that way. It also restricted credit growth somewhat. But despite this, money supply growth blew out because of the much larger than expected private
capital inflow. This year the Budget strategy was the same, that is, to increase the Budget domestic surplus of $1 billion so as to take more money out of the economy and to keep a tight hold on domestic credit growth as an offset to the money supply increase from large private capital inflow.
So what this policy of last year and of this year amounts to is increased taxes on Australian taxpayers and a reduction in government expenditure on their behalf with credit more difficult for them to obtain so as to offset the money supply increase coming from increased capital inflow. That is the essence of the policy. Australians are thereby paying a real cost for this massive uncontrolled inflow in the form of increased taxes, reduced government expenditure on essential programs and more limited and costly access to credit. All this so that this mammoth private capital inflow for purposes unknown can enter the country. It is truly quite an astonishing policy. It is also a dangerous policy because it builds the Government's economic strategy around a highly volatile and unreliable factor. If capital inflow falls well below its expected level it clearly creates great monetary problems because, being a major element in the expected money supply growth, .if the capital inflow falls short of the expected level then there is a shortfall in the money supply growth. Given that the Government's money supply target is estimated by some of the banks to be difficult to reach even if capital inflow occurs at the level expected, that is the $7.8 billion, then a shortfall would creaty real problems. This is exactly what now seems 11kely to occur.
Indications from the first two months' figures for this financial year are that the balance of payments current account deficit will be substantially higher than that estimated in the Budget and that the level of private capital inflow will be well below the estimated level in the Budget. In July and August the balance of payments on current account deficit averaged $750m a month com-
pared with theBudget estimate of $S60m a

Private Capital Inflow

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1389

month. Private capital inflow averaged $400m a month compared with a Budget estimated average of $650m a month. So in those two months, both the capital inflow and the current account deficit were running well below, far below, the budgeted levels. Of course this may not continue for the whole of the financial year. That is the essence of what the Treasurer has been saying in Question lime. But certainly for the first two months of the year the indications are that both capital inflow and the current account deficit are running in quite different ways from that which was estimated in the Budget, with resultant severe implications, if that continues, with regard to both the balance of payments exchange rate and the money supply. As a result of these developments money supply growth for the capital inflow has been way below expectations. In the month of July M3 grew by less than half the 10 per cent minimum target level. It is likely to be similar or even less in August. We do not have the figures yet. Hence the Reserve Bank yesterday moved to increase the trading bank limit on advances from 10 per cent to 12 per cent, which is a step towards offsetting the shortfall in money supply growth from the diminished capital inflow.
Certainly, further such action can be taken by the monetary authorities but undoubtedly, if current trends continue, there will have to be a complete reversal of the Budget policy, a policy that, on the face of what has happened in the first two months, is already clearly tottering. If we need a reversal of Budget strategy we will need tax cuts or we will need increases in government expenditures, or both, to ensure that there is not a substantial credit squeeze with rising interest rates resulting from this key element of the Budget's economic strategy, that is, the impact on the money supply of private capital inflow actually being realised.
Indeed, given the inevitability of lags in adjusting to the capital inflow impact on the money supply, it is highly likely, if current trends continue, that a tight credit situation with pressure for high interest rates will arise anyway. This is because the lag in government response, whether it be through the monetary policy or through the budgetary policy, is likely to mean that there will be a deterioration in the impact on the money supply through the diminution in capital inflow, arid particularly so if there is a dramatic shortfall in capital inflow. The fact that it is so volatile also makes it likely that such a lag will occur because the authority is not sure that a change in policy will be confounded by a sudden rapid rise in capital inflow and will tend to hesitate before it takes the appropriate offsetting action.

What all this shows is the acute danger of building a Budget strategy around the policy of accommodation to whatever level of capital foreign investors like to bring in. That is what we are doing. We are saying: 'We should not try to do anything to upset the inflow of foreign capital. If foreign investors want to bring $8 billion here this year, so be it. We will have to take all the offsetting actions which are necessary to ensure that this does not blow the economy sky high in one way or the other, and we have to change all of our tax strategies, our exchange rate strategies and so on to accommodate that inflow'. What an incredible kind of policy that is. Rather, in our opinion, we
should be seeking to control the capital inflow so that what comes in accords with what is in the best interests of consistent economic policy and does not impose severe costs on the people of the
nation.
These capital inflow factors also impinge on the exchange rate. A high level of capital inflow clearly has an effect on the exchange rate by building up reserves and so encouraging the appreciation of the exchange rate. This is what happened last year. The enormous rise in capital inflow provided a basis on which appreciation of the exchange rate could occur without any reduction in reserves. The appreciation of exchange rate last year was almost 10 per cent, and that has clear implications for both exporting and import competing industries. It reduces returns to export rural and mining industries, it increases the costs for export secondary industries-it makes it more difficult for them to export-and it reduces the cost of imports, thereby intensifying competition for import competing industries. The effect of all that, of course is to worsen our balance of payments.
The substantial appreciation of the Australian dollar last year, made possible by the large private capital inflow, thus has helped to bring about the deterioration in the balance of payments current account to which the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) drew attention a couple of days ago, pointing out that it in fact is much worse than we originally thought it would be, given the Budget figures. Now we find that the large capital inflow is slowing down so that with the adverse balance of payments current account which the revaluations have helped to bring about we now have
quite an adverse balance of payments.
We see little publicity but the fact is-a very important fact it is-that in the first two months of this year our international reserves fell by $1,OOOm, a fall of $1 billion in two months. This was partly because of devaluation of our gold assets. That accounted for $400m. The other

1390 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Private Capital Inflow

$600m loss in our reserves was caused by the deterioration in our balance of payments. As a'fesuit of these developments, appreciation of the Australian dollar has come to an end, at least for the time being .and for the foreseeable future. The trade weighted index of the Australian dollar is now at the same level as at the end of July and it has come down 0.5 per cent since mid-August. If the balance of payments current account and capital inflow levels continue as at present there will be a heavy loss of reserves, and a substantial revaluation is certain to have to occur, thus introducing a further element of inconsistency and changeability into government economic:policy. This exchange rate experience once again demonstrates the absurdity of allowing highievels of uncontrolled capital inflow to occur with consequent adjustments being made necessary to the rest of the economy.
The lesson of this whole episode is clearly that reliance by governments on large scale capital inflow is dangerous and unwise. It imposes considerable costs on the populace, whose living standards are reduced to accommodate it. It causes damaging structural changes to our exporting and importing competing interests, it introduces a high degree of variability and uncertainty into economic policy, and it allows an even greater proportion of our industry and resources to be owned and controlled by foreigners. What is clearly needed is a policy by which we control the level of foreign capital inflow. We should not accommodate the whole of the economy to whatever level of capital private investors seek to bring into this country. We must ensure that private capital inflow is used for the benefit of the nation. We should know exactly where it is going. We should not bring it in and then try to find out what is happening to it and make all the various adjustments that have to be made to accommodate the volatile nature of this private capital inflow. The lesson of the last year and a half is absolutely clear. If we continue in this way great damage will be brought to this nation by the Government's policies of uncontrolled capital inflow.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired. .
Mr HOWARD (Bennelong Treasurer) (3.33)-1 wish to take issue with the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) in regard to his. very last sentence and say that the lesson, if anything, of the last 18 months is that without the volume of private capital inflow this country experienced we would not have had the economic growth which this country experienced over that

period. The most singular feature of the honourable member's speech was the total absence of any explanation as to where the investment for the economic growth that, presumably, he and his party support would be found unless this country continues to attract a sufficient volume of overseas capital. The fact is that this country does not have enough domestic savings to generate the investment capital to fund development and to fund everything that Australians want to do.
We can keep foreign capital out if we want to. That is our option. It is an option that is clearly available to us and it is an option that clearly would be embraced by members of the Australian Labor Party if the honourable member for GelIibrand had his way. But the cost of keeping foreign capital out of Australia is that we would have to settle for a lower level of economic growth. We would have to settle for fewer jobs and for lower living standards. If that proposition was not demonstrated to be true and if it is not accepted on coming from the lips of the spokesmen for the Government, I invite honourable members just to contemplate what the honourable member for Gellibrand has just said. He devoted 15 minutes to bewailing the Government's policies and to criticising our reliance on private capital inflow from overseas but not a minute was spent telling the House where we would get the investment capital to provide all the activity. Where would Mr Wran get all that capital to fund all that economic development that he is so happy to talk about in the State of New South Wales? Where would governments anywhere around Australia get that capital?
Of course, a certain amount of money is available from domestic sources, but it cannot be denied that the domestic savings base in Australia is not sufficient to support the degree of development that has occurred and is in prospect of occurring in this country. That is not to say that policies ought not to be changed to encourage Australian investors to put more money into risk ventures. I for my part, as is the Government generally, am in favour of moving in that direction. I made that point plain in 'the Budget Speech. We would like to see what the report of the Committee of Inquiry into the Australian Financial System has to say about it. I put it very strongly that even with that occurring and even with greater incentives for the small investor to share the ownership of resource development in Australia we will still need generous amounts of overseas capital. We can choose to.reject that; we can c!1oose to pull down the shutters; we can retreat into our xenophobia, indifference and

Private Capital Inflow

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1391

indecision as far as resource development is concerned. That is out option. We can do that if we want to, but there will be a cost. I do not mind the Australian Labour Party and others in the com-
munity pointing out that that is an option for Australia, but I entreat them to point out the costs of that option. I entreat them to acknowledge that the cost of that option is slower development, lesser economic growth, fewer jobs and lower living standards. If we are to have a sensible debate on this issue, I think we have to recognise these facts.
Another remarkable thing about the speech of the honourable member for Gellibrand was that I had very great difficulty, until the concluding part of it, in really establishing whether he thought it was a good thing or a bad thing that there might not be the same volume of private capital inflow in the currt<nt financial year as was estimated at the time the Budget was brought down. This debate gives me an opportunity of going over some of the issues on this matter that arose during Question Time. The fact is that private capital inflow during July and August of this financial year has not been as high as it was during the concluding months of the last financial year. Of course, if we multiply by 12 or by 6, as the honourable member for Gellibrand has done, we do not get the figure that he and I agree, I think, was the effect of what was contained in the Budget Speech. But I remind him, as I reminded the House at Question Time, that private capital inflow for July and August is significantly above-about 46 per cent or 48 per cent abovewhat it was in July and August of the past financial year.
Of course, I make the obvious comment that one ought not to rely too heavily on two months figures to try to predict what the outcome for 12
months is going to be. Trying to predict private capital inflow is a notoriously difficult thing to do. I can remember responding less than two years ago to matters of public importance brought forward by the honourable member for Gellibrand about the volume of official borrowings by Australia to supplement our reserves. I can remember the honourable member for Gellibrand saying then that the strategy of trying to create a climate favourable for private capital inflow into Australia was not going to work. He was saying then that our economic strategy was in total tatters, and that we would have to go on borrowing and borrowing, month after month, and year after year on official account to supplement our reserves. Of course, that did not prove to be the case either. We witnessed last year and the year before, but particularly last year, a very significant return

to the historic reliance of this country on private capital inflow to finance a very large current account deficit. That was the situation that prevailed through the 1960s. It was the situation that prevailed until overseas investment confidence in this country collapsed in the first half of the 1970s. It is a policy to which this Government has worked and to which our policies have been directed in the 5-4years that it has been in office. We recognise the reality that even with an improved climate for domestic savings and greater incentives for the small investor, it will still be necessary to attract large amounts of overseas investment.
I, for one, do not feel embarrassed about the fact that this country has attracted large amounts of foreign investment. I, for one, do not believe we
have the luxury of picking and choosing to quite the extent the honourable member for Gellibrand thinks. We have always been very happy to take foreign investment when we could not get Australian companies to take the risk. We were happy in the 1940s to take foreign investment to start a motor manufacturing industry in this country. We have been happy to have foreign investment to start other industries in this country. We ought to recognise the contribution that foreign investment has made, not only to economic development more generally, but also to the creation of jobs for Australians and the enormous economic benefits all round for the Australian community.
This does not mean that the Government adopts a 'tickle-my-tummy' attitude towards foreign investment. The truth is that from the time the foreign investment policy was laid down in 1976 we have stuck to it. We have insisted that the 50 per cent equity guideline be adhered to as far as new resource projects are concerned. If I have come under any criticism over the last 18 months on the administration of foreign investment policy, and in particular regarding individual decisions, it has been criticism from people in governments around Australia to the effect that I have taken too hard a line in fighting for a share of Australian equity in resource projects. Of course, I, and the Government generally, make no apology for that. I believe the objectives and the implementation of our foreign investment policy strike a very sensible balance between the need to
have an attractive investment climate in this country and at the same time to ensure that Australians have a fair share of the action, particularly in resource projects.
The honourable member for Gellibrand wandered into the areas of the management of the exchange rate and the lending guidance of the Reserve Bank. I take the opportunity of repeating

1392 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Private Capital Inflow

again that the announcement made by the Reserve Bank last night that the growth in outstandings of the major trading banks has been lifted, as far as its guidance is concerned, from 10 per cent to 12 per cent does not in any way represent, as the honourable member for Gellibrand and others seem to think, some kind of dramatic reaction to what has occurred. It represents a sensible fine tuning of the situation by the Reserve Bank. It is the role of a central bank in our system to react flexibly to changing circumstances, and I think it would be a very odd and deficient central bank were it not prepared, in the face of changing monetary conditions, to effect some change in the lending guidance that is given to trading banks throughout Australia.
The talk of the honourable member for Gellibrand about a credit squeeze and the unavailability of credit is unfortunately all too familiar. When we have high volume private capital inflow there are threats of credit squeezes. When that situation changes, according to the measurement of the Opposition, one thing which does not change is the threat of a possible credit squeeze. Every year that I have been Treasurer we have had from the Opposition prophesies of doom and credit squeezes. Every single year we
have that same talk from the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) and from his colleagues about our management of the monetary situation. But somehow or other it has not occurred in the time we have been in government. Somehow or other what the honourable member for Gellibrand said on this subject had a rather hollow ring, as did some of the remarks of his leader at Question Time and more generally.
It is true that the private capital inflow for the first part of this financial year is lower than we had anticipated. Nonetheless, it is significantly higher than in the same period last year. Our
export performance for the first two months of this year has been more subdued, as the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) pointed out the other night. Some of that unfortunately has been due to the effects of strikes. Some of the very significant rise in imports, as I pointed out at Question Time, has been due to essentially one-off effects of the importation of a number of very large items, particularly in the transport area. Indeed, in the month of August deliveries of aircraft were valued at $131m which made a very significantly abnormal contribution to the import figures for that month. Of course, the extent to which expectations of private capital inflow and the surplus of private sector foreign exchange transactions made at the time of the Budget are realised remains to be seen as the year goes by.

What the honourable gentleman failed to appreciate in his remarks was that the management of the exchange rate, the volume of private capital inflow and the volume of imports are all critically interrelated. We cannot just address one, as he seemed to want to do, and forget about the consequences of that elsewhere. Obviously if we are to have a high volume of private capital inflow we have to adopt an appropriate exchange rate policy, otherwise we will have significant inflationary consequences. We are now having a high volume of private capital inflow in this country because we need the money to support resource and other development which, I understand in its quieter moments, the Australian Labor Party says it supports. The Australia Labor Party certainly would regard itself as being in the vanguard of those in the community who support greater economic growth and the greater provision of jobs.
But if we believe in development and in economic growth we have to accept that we need the money to fund that development and that growth. If we do not have a large enough volume of domestic savings to provide it we have to take it from overseas or we cannot have economic growth and development. We cannot have it all ways. We cannot have high economic growth, higher living standards, low private capital inflow, totally insulated domestic industries, and an undervalued exchange rate. If we try to have all of those things something will give and there will be a crunch before very long. I think the great deficiency of the contribution of the honourable member for Gellibrand in this debate was an all too great willingness on his part to believe that one could defy those laws of gravity and have all of those things at once.
Mr JACOBI (Hawker) (3.47)-1t is with pleasure that I second the motion moved by the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis). Let us be blunt about this Government's foreign investment policies and, in particular, those of the Treasurer (Mr Howard). The Treasurer's view seems to me to be in complete contrast to that of the Foreign Investment Review Board which believes, to some degree, in the control of foreign investors and the levels of equity. In the Treasurer's answers to repeated questions he has shown himself to be opposed to any limitations on foreign investment. It is an open slather approach. I will repeat some of the questions I asked him last night. Firstly, is it estimated that there will be an inflow of foreign capital into Australia of between $6 billion and $7 billion this year? The answer is yes. The Treasurer said so in the Budget Papers. If so, will a high proportion of this sum be speculative

Private Capital Inflow

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1393

capital over which the Government will have little control and no measure of its cumulative effect once it is within Australia? The answer is yes. I will qualify that later. The Treasurer does not know. But the answer is that it will be speculative. The Treasurer does not know what it will do. Secondly, has the Government deliberately abandoned the regular compilation of all statistics in this field, including the periodic surveys that played an important role in monitoring the levels of foreign ownership and control in Australia? The answer is yes. That is where the deficiency lies. Thirdly, is any consistent collection of data being undertaken relating to foreign ownership and control within the many sectors of the Australian economy? The answer is no. Fourthly, has his attention been drawn to the concern of the Minister for Agriculture (Mr Nixon) over the high level of foreign ownership of rural holdings in Australia? I trust that his attention has been drawn to this concern. Finally, is he able to say whether there is a very high level of foreign ownership of capital city property development throughout Australia? If so, is he able to say what degree of foreign ownership is the result of the high level of speculative capital over the last three years? The answer is yes. It is the result of a deficiency on the part of this Government.
Let us look at the effect of foreign investment on the rest of the economy. Does the Treasurer concede, for instance, that there was a huge increase in the value of borrowings by foreign controlled corporations from 1977-78 to 1978-79? If these figures are related to the total level of savings in the economy it is seen that Australian borrowings by foreign controlled corporations rose dramatically from 1.4 per cent in 1977-78 to 18.8 per cent in 1978-79 and 20 per cent in 1979-80. In fairness, these figures indicate the degree to which foreign investors are squeezing out domestic investors, home builders, and the like. The result has been a fall in consumer credit. The Treasurer agrees with that. In real terms, lending has fallen by about a quarter over the past four years, for instance, for the purchase of motor vehicles. Also, the number of loans for the purchase of new dwellings has fallen over the last four years by almost 6,000. An article in the Australian Financial Review headed 'Chinese property investors come in from the cold' states:
In this financial year almost $63 million worth of acquisitions have been revealed.
This figure, however, does not include commitments behind the investments, which are almost four times greater.
Amounts excluded are:
Close to $250 million in redevelopment and renovation costs.

An article in the Melbourne Age newspaper of 20 August headed 'PM acts on foreign land spree' states:
FIRB figures show that last year 114 properties, worth more than $70 million, were approved for purchase by the board. Authorities in several States believe the real level of foreign ownership could be higher, and growing as the strengthening Australian dollar attracts speculative investment from West Germany, other European countries and the Arab States.
A report in the National Times states:
Between April 1976 and December 1980, these investors spent almost $200 million acquiring approximately 10 million hectares of Australian rural land. But these figures account only for land purchases approved by the Treasury's Foreign Investment Review Board . . . during that period.
In recent months, concern has been expressed in political circles that foreigners have also bought considerable tracts of rural land through dummy Australian companies which do not have to seek approval for land purchase from the FIR B.
It has been estimated that more rural land has been bought up in the last six months than in the whole of the preceding 12 months. No wonder people in the rural areas are becoming concerned at this high level of penetration in rural holdings. We must compile statistics that reintroduce, together with the periodic surveys that we used to undertake, an adequate basis upon which to assess the level of foreign ownership and control in this country.
I turn now to some policy issues-I trust that they are different from the policies of the Treasurer-which at least are constructive. We ought to follow the path that most European nations, Japan and other Asian countries now follow. We should develop our corporations with minimal dependence on foreign equity. We must use our strength as a major supplier of raw materials to strike a better bargain for foreign investment proposals. We must insist on technology transfer to Australian control as the price for the exploitation of our resources. We must insist on further downstream processing, and research and development, within Australia. But we must have a government with the forethought and understanding, and the will to make the decisions. Why does the Treasurer not make those the criteria for the Foreign Investment Review Board for the entry of foreign capital into this country? They ought to be a must.
The Treasurer went so far as to say that we ought to acknowledge the three Australian parties involved in the negotiation of foreign investment proposals, namely, the Federal Government, the State governments and local companies. Regrettably, often they do not work together and are readily played off against one another by foreign corporations. In other words, Australia gains the

1394 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Private Capital Inflow

lowest common multiple in any foreign investment. What makes the situation worse is that Federal and State governments not only readily acquiesce to foreign investment packages but also they are unconcerned about the long term implications. A classic example was the recent outburst by the Minister for Lands in Queensland because of the Foreign Investment Review Board's rejection of an application for foreign takeover of some of the most prestigious rural land in Queensland.
