Mr JACOBI-I do. It just confirms the point that there is a problem. What we have to facewhat the Treasurer fails to face-is simply that the world-wide economic slowdown has once more brought the eyes of foreign investors to look avidly at new resource opportunities in Australia where attractions, presently unparalleled in the world, stand out. The situation was different five to six years ago. We have enormous low cost mineral deposits awaiting development, low cost energy available for mineral processing and the support, offering from State governments, for infrastructure needs. We are wide open to be exploited. The deficiency is that we do not have the guidelines or the criteria to close the gap.
I will briefly raise to more points. Within Australia the amount of exploitation and mining being done by foreign government owned organisations is rising at a rapid rate. It is marvellous to see headlines in glowing terms when Japanese, British or French government agencies get involved in Australian mining projects. Let us contrast this with the attitude of the Treasurer, who gets very emotional when the Opposition asks the Government to take a stand or responsible decisions in the mining sector in Australia. Quite frankly, it is a hypocritical approach. We have seen it with the uranium deposit in Queensland. The Chairman of Energy Resources of Australia bragged about the bargain price that he got for the Government's share in the Ranger deposit. It was an outrage! Why does the Government get upset about those sorts of things? In every other country in the world, particularly in Western countries, and now Canada, the Government has found that it has an obligation--
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MilIar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.

Mr HYDE (Moore) (3.56)-The honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) spoke of the problems of foreign ownership and the monetary and exchange rate implications of high levels of private capital inflow. I note that he does not seem to be worried about the effects of public sector capital inflow. For that reason I will confine my remarks to private sector capital inflow. It was not clear to me whether he was most worried about the fact that capital inflow was high or
about the fact that perhaps it would not be as high in the coming 12 months. At different stages in his speech he seemed to emphasise different points. It should be remembered that Australia has been a net capital importer in almost every year since its discovery. If it were not for capital inflow
Australia would still be a land of gum trees, boomerangs and spears. Capital inflow has enabled the development of the nation to this date. In the long run there is clearly a benefit from foreign investment; there is a benefit to Australia from giving people in Australia more capital and more tools to work with. That is what capital investment amounts to. It gives Australians more resources to earn more wealth for themselves. As I have said,
Australia always has been dependent upon that inflow.
The flow of money into the country must be matched by the flow of real goods. The term 'bal-
ance of payments' is not an accidental term. Payments must balance. A suplus on capital account must necessarily be balanced by a deficit on current account. Whether on trade account or invisibles, it must be matched. There is no point in building up reserves overseas. The only purpose of those reserves is to import things. Incidentally, people who still talk of the benefits of a resources boom but who, at the same time, set up barriers to stop imports, are talking nonsense. We cannot refuse to import goods and have the benefits of a resources boom. Inherently it is not on. The benefits from the resources boom will flow to us from things that we export, that build up our balance of payments and allow us to import goods and services that we will use and that will increase our standard of living.
The last two months' tJgures might indicate a lower rate of capital inflow than would be the average for the whole 12-month period. They are 50 per cent higher than they were for the same two months last year. However, any two months' figures for something as volatile as capital inflow tells us almost nothing. Those two months were affected by major items' in the trade account, namely the purchase of Boeing aircraft for domestic airlines and by a reduction in exports-also on the trade account-affected by a strike in

Private Capital Inflow

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1395

Queensland. It is inevitable that one month's figures will not tell us very much about 12 months' figures. I think people who are trying to predict a l2-month outcome from them are really whistling in the wind.
Let us assume that that was the condition for the whole 12 months and that we did not get the level of capital inflow that we had hoped we would because that level of capital inflow would have enabled Australia to command greater resources. It would be far from the disruptive tragedy the honourable member for Gellibrand suggested. If private exchange transactions do not have the monetary effects that we anticipate, then we can take official action to bring about a money supply outcome that is desired. The Australian Wheat Board can borrow its $2,400m odddepending on the size of the harvest-from the Australian capital markets or it can borrow from the rural credits department of the Reserve Bank. What it does makes a vast difference to the money supply. It can borrow overseas. Semi-government authorities can borrow overseas or they can borrow domestically. The Commonwealth can redeem some debfand thus offset the heavy borrowings of the States and the semi-government authorities. All of those courses of action are available to keep money supply on track. They if necessary will be used. The data come in quickly enough so that they can be used. As far as the management of the money supply is concerned, capital flow need not be disruptive. Certainly, as the member for Gellibrand notes, if we have a smaller inflow of capital from overseas, whether on official or private account, it will have exchange rate implications. If the exchange rate is lowered or caused to rise less rapidly, that fact will improve the competitive position of Australia's exporting industries and its import competing industries. So in part the so-called problem is self-adjusting. There is also an immediate benefit to a section of the Australian community that in part offsets the advantage that the Australian community would have had from the inflow of goods and services that would have matched an inflow of capital.
It is rather foolish to suggest that predictions rather than elements of the Budget determined by the Government are part of government policy. If there is a prediction about an item that is inherently volatile it is foolish to suggest that the Budget strategy is coming apart. It is rather foolish scaremongering. There is no reason why the Budget strategy should come apart. There is powerful little indication at this stage that it will come apart.

Much has been said about the foreign ownership of Australian resources. Australia forms a little more capital per head than the average of the countries in the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development. What happens in Australia is that capital finds its way largely into the housing market. This must be one of the very best housed nations in the world. To the extent that we have a foreign ownership problem, it is because we are directing our capital into housing. Is the Opposition advocating that we should not channel capital in that direction? Is it saying that we should not have a home savings grant? Is it saying that interest rates for housing loans should be higher? If so, it should say so explicitly. But it ought not attempt to have its cake and eat it. It ought to come down in one camp or the other. I will come down in a camp and say that we should let the market determine interest rates. If it did more of our resources would be available for long term industrial development. That would divert wealth from my generation to my children's generation. I believe that would be a good thing. The Foreign Investment Review Board ensures that people who invest in Australia do not get their investments on the cheap. It is important to ensure that the investment that comes into Australia is of value to Australians.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MilIar)- Order! The discussion is now concluded.
NEW PARLIAMENT HOUSE: CONSTRUCTION AND FITTING-OUT
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Leader of the House) (4.7) - I move:
(I) That, for the purpose of sub-section 8 (2) of the Parliament House COllstructioll Authority Acl 1979, the House of Representatives authorizes the commencement of work on the following declared stage in the construction of Parliament House, namely, the construction and fitting-out of the building that is to constitute Parliament House, together with~
(a) the construction, on the site of Parliament House, of access roads, parking areas and other facilities to be provided on that site in connection with Parliament House; and
(b) the landscaping of that site.
(2) That a message be sent to the Senate acquainting it of the resolution agreed to by the House of Representatives.
This motion represents the decision by this House to proceed with the construction of the new Parliament House. There has been, or course, some discussion in this House in recent weeks about the proposal. It has not been in any depth but there have been many opportunities for honourable members to look at the construction and fittingout of the Parliament House, to discuss details and to examine the design material that has been on display in the Parliament House, and of course, to meet with the members of the Parliament

1396 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

New Parliament House

House Construction Authority, the Archi.tects and others to determine the extent to which the proposals meet with their own ideas and conform to what they expect to be the requirements of this Parliament which, without doubt, will be meeting the needs of this country for many years to come.
The approval of this motion by the House this afternoon will allow the Parliament House Construction Authority, in fact, to proceed with the construction of the new Parliament House. While a great deal of work has been undertaken in clearing and preparing the site on Capital Hill, until this motion is passed no final approval will have been given for the construction. So this motipn is of tremendous importance. It has quite an historical significance apart from also, it is hoped, attending to the more practical and immediate needs of members of this place, many of whom I know live and work in circumstances which are not suitable for performing the duties that are expected of them in today's environment.
Honourable members will recall that the House did agree, on 20 August last, to a motion which defined the construction and fitting-out of Parliament House to be a declared stage in the terms of the Parliament House Construction Authority Act 1979. Agreement to the motion moved today will allow work to commence on that declared stage. I have mentioned that honourable members have had the opportunity to examine the design material displayed in this building and to study the reports of the Construction Authority and of the Joint Standing Committee on the New Parliament House. I hope that any honourable members who have any specific interest in the proposals have done that. I hope that they have had the opportunity to talk with the architects and others who have a completely up to date knowledge of the proposals as they are now put forward.
Approval for the declared stage will allow the Authority to commence w<;>rk on the foundations, the basements, the plant rooms and the forecourt together with the construction of basic engineering services. I am advised that the Authority will also purchase some major pieces of equipment such as cranes. Currently, the Construction Authority has approval from the Parliament to commence the preparation of the detailed design of the new Parliament House, including specifications and tender documents, and the preparation and excavation of the site. These approvals were given, as I mentioned, in August 1980 following acceptance of the competition winner's design.
The building budget for the project, at current prices, is $303.9m. That is made up of the budget that was approved by Parliament in August 1980

of $220m in May 1978 prices. When inflation is added for the period from May 1978 to June 1981, the figure is augmented by a sum of $76m. Changes requested by the Joint Standing Committee and by the Government involve a further $7.9m, bringing the total amount to $303.9m at June 1981 prices. The project is, therefore, within the budget that was set down. It is hoped that the very excellent work that has been undertaken by the Parliament House Construction Authority, the architects and the builders can proceed so that the present rate of achievement is maintained in the future. Certainly, my advisers tell me that the Authority is determined to maintain the budget. That is an objective that is to be much
commended.
There have been a number of design changes. Mr Speaker, I know that you are very well aware of them. But it might be worth identifying them
for the purposes of consideration by the Parliament. The first change is in the field of accommodation. There have been some increases in the area of the post office and also some increases in office accommodation for senators and members and for other officers of the Parliament. Some additional seating has also been provided for in the Senate gallery. I am told that the estimated cost of those modifications for accommodation is just short of $lm,-$0.9m. Secondly, there has been some modification to the refreshment services area. Increased dining facilities for the public and staff have been provided for. A facility is to be established so that there can be room service for senators and members. Those who live and work in this place know how unsatisfactory it is to see trays and items of food and drink left around the corridors. There is so little scope for such facilities in this Parliament House. Provision has been made for refreshment facilities in senators' and members' suites. The package of those changes in
that area is estimated to cost about $2.75m.
A close examination has been made of security
in the building by the security advisers to the Government and to you, Mr Speaker. As a result, there has been some relocation of high risk areas, such as parking and uqloading facilities, from under the building. Indee.d, it is tragic that those changes are necessary for' security reasons. When one looks at the world around us it is understandable. Although we have been extraordinarily fortunate in this country to date, I think we would be hiding our heads in the sand if we did not take the advice of those who give advice in this area. Consequently, it has been necessary to relocate some of those facilities. Communications and other facilities for the management of national emergencies 'have also been re-examined. A

New Parliament'House

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1397

number of additional security control points have been provided at all building entrances and intersections of different circulation routes. The estimated cost of those changes amounts to some $5.25m.
At the same time, there have been some savings. There has been a cost offset by the raising of the base of the building and by the removal of what has been identified as the notional defence shelter. As a 'result, there is a saving of about $lm. The budget, therefore, takes account of those factors.
There are some non-building items which are still to be approved but which need to be identified at this time so that there is a recognition that there will be an added cost for those items. They include furniture, broadcasting and, telephone equipment, security equipment, art works, et cetera. The figure that has been given to me for those is about an additional $82m. Those items have not been included in the budget to date. Of course, a lot will depend on what exact facilities are required, what can be utilised frQm this place and to what degree new equipment necessitates changes that are not presently sufficiently identified to make it possible to cost them accurately. If honourable members are looking at the total cost of the package, however, they should realise that these non-building items will certainly be essential for the operation of the Parliament and will have to be added to the budget cost.
The schematic design is the basis for approval of the declared stage. It represents a year's work by the architect who, with other consultants, has spent an enormous amount of effort and time on that stage. I believe that he and those with him deserve to be complimented on the work that has been done. Certainly, the schematic design has now been examined very closely by the Construction Authority, members of this place, all user departments, and the Joint Standing Committee, of course. It has been on display in the House of Representatives billiard room since 18 August 1981. If any honourable member has not had the opportunity to have a look at it it is still on display there. The completion of the developed design is scheduled for May 1982. This will, of course, then be available for examination by honourable members and user departments. Ultimately it will go to the Joint Standing Committee for approval.
This year the Budget allocation for the Authority is $26.3m. This comprises $14m for construction work and $12.3m for ongoing design work, site excavation and the administration cOsts of the Parliament I-;lbuse Constfucti6n Authority.

This was the total amount sought by the Authority for the 1981-82 financial year. In terms of funding and the work that has been undertaken, there is no reason why the schedule that has been set-it is hoped that the building will be available for occupation and opening in the bicentenary year-can be achieved.
The figures given to me concerning progress on the site might be of interest to honourable members. I am told that currently some 850,000 cubic. metres of soil and rock of a total of approximately 1,000,050 cubic metres have been excavated. The bulk of the rock and soil has been redistributed over the site to create a level building platform. Those who know the old C?pital Hill will have noted with some amazement how this has certainly changed the physical nature of that hill. One wonders whether, with the new building on it, it will ever be quite the same again. Of course, it will not be. I believe that this concept of a parliament house will add very significantly to the focal point of this national capital and I believe, like so many others before me, it will very fittingly serve the democratic needs of this country for many years to come.
Only a relatively small amount of the rock and soil that has been excavatedhas been removed from the site and used as infill on other projects; mucb of it, as I have explained, is being redistributed to create a level building platform for the new Parliament House. I am advised that the Construction Authority is ready to let tenders for work in the forecourt area and that this will be advertised immediately this House and the Parliament has approved the project to the declared stage. The motion is, therefore, one of very considerable significance.
I thank you, Mr Speaker, the members of the Joint Standing Committee and other members of this place who have worked to try to ensure that the target dates are met and the designed framework is set on sche.dule. I believe the work has been such that honourable members will find the place far more suitable than this place to their requirements in the future and that it will be comparable with areas used by others in the community for conducting business or for pursuing their professions. When one looks at the enormous increase in the responsbilities that lie on members of parliament, the demands that are being placed on them and the degree to which there is a necessity for a constant contact with a wide range of diverse groups, from whateveI: side of the House we might come, I am sure that it is essential that the facilities are adequate. I believe that this motion will ensure that our objective of

1398 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

New Parliament House

achieving that by the bicentenary year is advanced more than somewhat. I commend the motion to the House.

It is worth remembering that this parliament house was designed by a committee. It is also worth remembering that, over the course of that 50 years, nationhood as a concept has grown immensely.

Throughout the course of the year that number ebbs and flows, depending on whether the House is sitting. Quite clearly, this building is inadequate to provide suitable working conditions for everybody in the building. MEAT EXPORT (PENALTIES) BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 10 September, on motion by Mr Nixon:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry)-Mr Speaker, may I have your indulgence to suggest that the House has a general debate covering this Bill, the Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Bill, the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Bill and the Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Bill as they are associated measures? Separate questions will, of course, be put on each of the Bills at the conclusion of the debate.
Mr SPEAKER-Is it the wish of the House to have a general debate covering these measures? There being no objection, I will allow that course to be followed.
Mr KERIN (Werriwa) (4.33)-The Opposition does not oppose these Bills. If I can go through them quickly, the first Bill I will be talking about is the Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Bill, which refers to contravention of licence conditions. Previously the penalty was $2,000. The penalty is now to be $100,000 or five years' imprisonment, or $5,000 or one year's imprisonment for matters that can be settled in minor courts. All of these Bills, of course, relate to the last two months of what has been commonly known as the horse meat scandal. I guess there will be quite a bit of discussion about that matter yet, but at least this is a fair indication of the strength with which the Government feels on this matter. These are very large penalties, and if they had been in force before perhaps some of the actions that have taken place would not have occurred. In all fairness, no one would really have thought that anyone would be so irresponsible as to prejudice Australia's meat trade by doing what obviously has been done.
I will be speaking mainly about the first three Bills. The fourth Bill, the Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Bi,!l will be dealt with by my colleague the honourable member for Parramatta (Mr John Brown). The second Bill I will be speaking about is the Meat Export (Penalties) Bill. This is really a separate Bill because at present the matters to which this Bill refers in terms of penalties are covered entirely by regulations. The penalty. there, with respect to the regulations, is $100. In the regulations themselves

the penalties have been raised to $1,000. Regulations 33A, 58 and sub-regulation 59 (1) relate to the misuse of inspection stamps, alteration of descriptions and tags, or making false or misleading statements. Again, the penalty will now be $100,000 or five years. I point out here, as I did recently in a censure motion, that this matter was flagged by the report of the Kelly Committee of Inquiry to Examine Commonwealth and State Meat Inspection Systems. The report was delivered in January 1980, and on page 41, it states:
It was observed that at several works visited, works management held the interstate branding stamps. The stamp was applied by an employee and the works merely paid the Fee. In other words, no re-inspection occurred. We were also told that, in many iF not all cases, the 'Australia Approved' stamp was provided by works management, and iF a new stamp was required, the inspectors obtained it From management. This practice should cease.
It is a great pity that the Government did not act on that recommendation. The regulations as they presently stand resulted in one fine being imposed on the company, W. Pope and Co. Pty Ltd. If the Government had realised then that penalties should be much higher perhaps it would have increased them at that time, but that did not occur. I recently put a question on notice stating that after 18 months investigation this firm was fined $100 yet in 1980 it received an export development grant of over $10,000. I do not know whether that grant was for initiative or incentive! It is ironic the way different government Acts actually work.
The third Bill I refer to is the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Bill which, as the name indicates, refers directly to a penalty for trade misdescription of meat. The penalty was $200. In keeping with the other penalties, it will be increased to $100,000 and/or five years gaol. These are very heavy penalties. The Opposition agrees with the Government levying such penalties because the extent of the crime is massive. We are overjoyed that the price of meat has not slumped to any large degree. The Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) answered a question without notice yesterday to that effect. There will
ot be many long term ramifications in this matter
that are immeasurable for which we can never really account. Trade wiil be hurt and prices will drop. We hope that they will not drop by much and that the measures being taken will ensure the trade. The industry is valued at over $3.5 billion. It has a trade of over $800m per annum to the United States.
There have been many calls for retrospective penalties to deal with the.irresponsible people, the criminals, who have prejudiced this trarle. I wouid'

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1401

like those penalties to be made retrospective if we could actually get the charges to stick in that way but I doubt that the courts would uphold any move for retrospectivity in the criminal law. I point out that the people who will be eventually
caught as a result of the investigations under way, apart from the one person who has been arrested in connection with this matter, can be charged under the Trade Practices Act as it exists. It has maximum penalties of up to $50,000. The Government could also use the common law charges related to conspiracy to defraud and, therefore, exact some penalty that fits the crime. It is a very great crime indeed.
In the past two months of allegation and counter-allegation the media had a field day on this issue. Some of the stories caused me a lot of concern because they were unconfirmed. Even so,
there was a lot of substance to them. In the light of what has gone on over the past two months these Bills can still be shown to be only fiddling with the edges of far wider problems in the Australian meat industry. The Minister has decided to set up a royal commission. We back that decision. We
called for a royal commission a few weeks ago. We are not certain in our own minds, however, that a royal commission or any sort of inquiry will recommend what could not have been achieved by the effective exercise of power by various Ministers and departments and the proper support by relevant law enforcing authorities. The problem is not all with the Minister for Primary Industry who is at the table. It involves Commonwealth and State Ministers for agriculture and Commonwealth-State relations. It is a great pity that we have to have a royal commission to look into the industry due mainly to the Minister not taking enough action and the complexities of the system. One of the reasons the Minister was reticent to act at first is that the system to a large degree has relied on honour. When people are dishonourable honour systems break down completely. It is like a gentleman's club; the moment there are not gentlemen it is in a lot of trouble.
The role of the Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation has been criticised, I think in many cases unjustly. The Corporation's role is clearly set out. It grants licences under very strict requirements. The Australian Meat and Livestock CorpO.ration has been asking the industry for a long time to be allowed to enter works. It has to allocate the licences and, by and large, it has responsibility for meat from the ship on. I do not know what will come out of the inquiry but I hope that the royal commission recommends that the AMLC be allowed to enter works or have some

alternative. We hope that a single meat inspection service will come out of the inquiry. As I said in the debate on the censure motion that the Opposition moved in this House, the Minister has bungled on this issue. In all fairness, I can understand his initial reticence. No one would have thought that there was widespread scandal and fraud in the industry but as it developed it became
clear that very drastic action had to be taken. I am not saying this because of hindsight and I do not think it is very clever to say it but at the outset of the allegations or after a week or so when the Minister realised how widespread they were the meat inspectors, State departments of agriculture, meat producers and the AMLC itself should have been called together for a conference to see how quickly the existing loopholes could be plugged. It is very easy to go through the chain to see where all the loopholes are. That is a simple exercise.
I do not think that the security of beef will ever be guaranteed to the extent that the security of money is when it is transferred from a bank to a firm in a payroll but there are obvious loopholes which have to be closed. The process by and large
is that once stock arrives at an export abattoir for slaughter a veterinarian from the Department of Primary Industry conducts an ante-mortem, checking every animal for signs of disease and fitness for slaughter. Immediately each animal is slaughtered, the Department of Primary Industry inspectors begin their post-mortem inspections at all points along the slaughter chain from the knocking box to the final wash. Carcasses may be either stored in chillers and boned at the killing establishment or sent to independent secondary works for storage and boning. In the latter case carcasses must be accompanied by a DPI export transfer certificate. A certificate is also required if bone(,i out meat is to be put into cold storage at a location away from the boning works. DPI inspectors supervise the boning, packaging and sealing of meat into cartons which are stamped according to the Department's regulations. The cold storage of processed meat is also supervised by DPI personnel. The transport of carcasses or processed
meat must be accompanied by DPI export transfer certificates. Packaged meat in cartons is generally loaded into containers and DPI sealed at cold stores. This technically classifies it as being onboard for shipping. If meat is not transported to the dockside in containers, again a DPI certificate
and DPI supervision are required for the loading of the cartons.
To me this sequence of movements clearly indicates that the system is most vulnerable to exploi~
tation by criminal acts between the abattoir and secondary boning works, the boning room and the

1402 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

cold store and the cold store and the whaff. Most industry sectors believe the role played by the DPI is more than satisfactory, the number of inspectors involved being dependent on the volume of throughput and the availability of staff. If there are anomalies in any consignments DPI inspectors are bound not to accept them and to return them to their point of dispatch, Theoretically, the strict application of DPI check and recheck procedures leaves no room for malpractice but, as one meat processing company official recently put it, even in the best systems there are loopholes. Discussions with the AMLC have pointed out that export meat can be handled by three or four different cold stores before export. To fill a container about 600 cartons are required.' These could come from a number of locations. They could travel to Melbourne for shipment from Brisbane or Sydney. It would be easy to change cartons in transit. Whole cartons could be removed and replaced with others or cartons containing a different product could be added. Alternatively, empty cartons could be trucked along with packaged meat ready to accommodate a substitute at a predetermined location along the transit route. The people involved in shipment might know what had happened but they could plead ignorance and they would be telling the truth.
A further possibility for exploitation exists in the falsification of travel and product description documents. I think it was Protean Enterprises Pty Ltd, which leases the Profreeze boning room, which released a 10 point statement a couple of weeks ago to absolve itself of blame. It said that if horse meat had been put in cartons labelled 'Profreeze' it would have been done illegally off Protean premises and therefore out of its control. It said it was impossible for an outside company to prepare horse meat in a Profreeze boning room at Protean. Clearly, labels can be printed and meat replaced once a consignment leaves the works.
Eventually those sorts of loopholes in the chain were plugged by the Minister and there was a statement to that effect. But the thing which happened which alarmed the Opposition most was that it was virtually the United States Department of Agriculture which once again set the standards for our abattoirs in Australia for the new procedures to guarantee meat from Australia. It. did so by and large with respect to the species testing procedures which our meat now has to undergo. The Minister set up a royal commission. He announced measures to tighten up the loopholes which I have enumerated already. There will be a system of carton seals which will cost $1 m. We do not know quite what stage the royal commission is at and the regulations, which have

been tightened up, so far have not been notified by way of circulation to members in the usual way, but the Minister's announcement probably covers that point. The Minister also has announced some $6m for the species testing procedures. But I think these procedures will cost the industry more money. As the Minister said at the Estimates committee hearing for the Department of Primary Industry, it is probable that there will be payment back through the industry of the 50 per cent which already is involved in inspection charges.
It can be said that the royal commission would not have been necessary if the Government had acted quickly but there was disastrous publicity and some of it, as I have said already, was misleading. I think there was a fair amount of evidence of administrative timidity and a lack of political will, not just at the Commonwealth level but also at the State level. We have a bit of a problem here in that if there had not been any publicity people would have had all justification in saying that there was a coverup. So there are two arguments: Keep quiet and try to save the export industy or risk people's health and the guaranteeing of the trade. It is a conflict between keeping quiet or exposing a situation. I guess governments are always slow to act and in this case it was a very big problem. Again, I cannot blame the Minister for Primary Industry entirely. There was a massive problem in getting the State governments to agree. In the Kelly report to which I referred earlier, Mr Kelly says in the Epilogue:
Since the circulation of the draft report it has been made quite clear that, at departmental level, Victoria, New South Wales and Queensland will not accept any of these solutions. We are not so certain where Western Australia stands. We know that this Committee cannot persuade the State departments; this must be left to their political masters, pushed we hope and expect, by the meat industry in general and the producers in particular, all of whom agree at least on one thing, namely that they do not want to continue as at present. Yet that is what will happen if some heads are not knocked together.
Even as recently as two or three months ago, the industry still had not a,greed. The Meat Council had a different position from the Cattlemen's Union, and the State Ministers, as the Minister said at the Estimates. committee hearings yesterday, have been intransigent. There have beensome allegations by State Ministers that the Minister for Primary Industry was being a bit cavalier about funding. But regardless of where the truth of some of those things lies, it is quite certain that this is a terribly difficult system to achieve. The industry and the producers must put pressure on their State Ministers to get a single meat inspection system. The fact, for example, that the requirements of the United States Department of

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1403

Agriculture on species testing obliges the Commonwealth to go along simply means that the Commonwealth Government has to have control of the inspection system. It is quite certain that the Commonwealth is in the position where it must guarantee exports and therefore it should be the body that carries out the meat inspection. For some time the Government has not been attuned to the requests and the needs of the beef and meat industries. I will not go through the arguments with respect to the problems of the abattoirs and the live sheep industry. The Government has had to bailout one company to the extent of $750,000 and that company has gone bankrupt. Protean is in the market trying to get it-and there are other allegations about that company. But it is absolutely essential that the domestic market also is looked at. I recently put out a statement saying that it would be criminally irresponsible if the States did not go along with the Minister's plea to co-operate with the royal commission because it is quite evident that there should be equally high penalties for breaches in respect of domestic mislabelling. Just to give the House an idea of what is alleged to have been happening in Queensland, I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a copy of a speech by Mr Tom Burns, the honourable member for Lytton, in the Queensland Parliament.
Leave granted.
The speech read as follows-
I was going to continue in that vein but this morning the Leader of the Opposition took a very responsible stand on the meat processing problem. He asked the Premier whether, if he gave him a list of places in Queensland that were processing kangaroo meat-or horse or donkey or buffalo meat-and not dyeing it as is required under one section of the regulations, he would do anything about it. The Premier did everything but say'Yes'.
This morning I visited the Watson cold stores in my electorate, where I saw 2,580 plasic bags of undyed roo meat. It is there now. It is there for every honourable member to see. There is no dye in it at all. There are 172 cartons of branded roo meat and 2,580 bags of undyed roo meat in that cold store now. The bags have been recycled. Some bags previously contained a substance called nitrophil, which is associated with ammonium nitrate.
Let us see what happens to the meat. It comes into cold stores after being killed in State-Government-controlled slaughterhouses in country areas. Honourable members may know of the little slaughterhouses that kill up to 50 a week. Do they know what happens there? Government inspectors do not inspect the cattle when they are killed. When a fellow kills them he keeps the heart, the offal and other internal pieces that are necessary for inspection. He puts them away. The Government inspector in his regular trip around comes, maybe, two or three days later, by which time the carcasses have been eaten by the local community. That is the system the Government operates on. If the inspector comes only once a week or once a fortnight, horses, donkeys or kangaroos could be killed in that slaughterhouse and the Government would not know. If the meat is sneaked into Brisbane after 4

p.m. when the Commonwealth inspector is off duty, as long as it is addressed interstate for pet food it can go anywhere. Tomorrow it could be in anyone of the meat shops in this city, and there is no way that it could be checked out.
One of the great problems is the lack of single inspection; the weakness is the dual system that this Government is trying to prop up. Do honourable members know what was done as a test of the dual inspection system across the borders? A box full of ice-no meat at all-was packed and sent to New South Wales, down to Victoria, over to South Australia and on to Western Australia and then all the way back. It came back covered in re-inspection stamps and bills for the inspection-but it didn't have any meat in it! How could they have inspected it? How could they have charged for those meat inspections when the ice did the trip? When it came back it had so many inspection stamps on it that the name of the people it was sent to could hardly be read.
I will give a few of the names I collected when I was out at Watsons cold stores this morning. I mentioned them to the Leader of the Opposition and he took a very responsible attitude by saying to Joh, 'I'll give them to you privately if you will have a look at it.' All we wanted was to know where this undyed meat ended up. We are accusing no one! A mob called Lohn Gordon (or 'Cordon')-I,960 bags of unmarked roo meat from Charleville. The Minister for Tourism, National Parks, Sport and The Arts called out this morning, 'It's all dyed.' This is not dyed. I took my camera with me. I have photographs of it. It is not Watsons' fault. They just run a cold store. They just store whatever anyone brings there. They cannot be blamed.
There is a manipulation of the system brought about by lack of complete control after inspection. Each Tuesday night 20 t of roo meat, much of it undyed, goes out of the TNT depot in this State to Victoria. Every Friday night another 20 t goes out of the Buranda area to Victoria-some dyed and some undyed. How do we know where it goes? After it leaves here there is no inspection to see that it has arrived at pet-food places. Once it goes off to the alleged address of an interstate pet-food company, it could be delivered anywhere. Honourable members can go over to Anderson's old boning room at Riverside in Curtin Avenue, Hamilton; and see them boning kangaroos and other animals. This morning, C. F. Thomas Pty Ltd, a former Queensland primary products company, had two lots-221 bags and 390 bags-of undyed roo meat. A group called Tatiara had 90 undyed bags and 172 cartons branded 'roo meat'. They were the only ones I found that were branded as roo meat. I repeat that two 20 t consignments of roo meat leave this city each week to go off to the South.
I wondered why the Premier this morning would not take those names and say, 'Lt>t's go and have a look and see who they are selling it to.' Let him send someone over there now. The meat inspectors and the management of the cold stores will co-operate. They have nothing to hide.
Mr McKechnie interjected.
Mr BURNS: The honourable member for kangaroo killers is not worried about the beef industry.
Every time. I interjected on the Premier this morning he said, 'I am going to protect the export trade.' He did not say anything about the people of this city who eat meat or the rest of the people of Australia who make up the domestic market. Not once did that come up. We want to protect that trade, too, because a lot of meatworkers' jobs will go down the drain if this Government does not do something about its own inspection service. from Queensland to Victoria. State meat inspectors know about this, yet nothing has been done about it. At St George, roos are gutted and thrown back into the back of trucks. Mr KERIN-I thank the House and the Minister. In that speech Mr Burns made some very serious allegations indeed. I will read just one of them to the House. He said:

So, so much for the Queensland inspection system which the Premier of Queensland was recently defending. The point really is not that the public cannot eat kangaroo or horse or goat-I think I have eaten all three-but that it should know what it is eating and that it is safe to eat what it is eating. Senator Mulvihill recently asked a question about the extent of kangaroo meat exports for the 1980-81 financial year. He was told that 1,944,187 kilograms of kangaroo meat, fresh, chilled or frozen, suitable for human consumption and with a value of nearly $3.5m was exported. It is absolutely essential that we know that meat we are eating locally is fit for human consumption.
There is every chance, I think, that the export substitution, and particularly the horse meat substitution, eventually will be narrowed down to one group of operators with links to a few firms and individuals, and not much more. I really still have no evidence, despite the widespread allegations, that it does not go beyond one group of operators. I guess the basis for my putting forward that hunch is the economics of the whole thing. Last Thursday or Friday we heard the latest allegation; that some meat for domestic consumption in Victoria was found to be boxes full of stones and grass and all the rubbish. Surely to God anyone buying meat domestically is not going to accept that sort of stuff and actually pay money for it. It is all right killing horses in the Northern Territory and exporting them through Melbourne, but it must cost a heck of a lot of money to put them through that way. Local horse, if it is good horse, probably costs as much as beef in any case. So I just cannot see that we will get a heck of a lot of evidence for the economics of widespread

rackets in the export trade. We certainly need to find out about it. I think we can say that in the export of meat there has always been a situation whereby, when there has been a squeeze, people have fallen short of their orders and they have done a bit of substitution. But probably there has been a substitution of other cuts, if substituton at all, of meat fit for human consumption. The domestic matter is another thing altogether. I think it is absolutely essential that the States co-operate and that we catch up with the breaches here; otherwise people in Australia will have no confidence in Australian meat. There is no way that the Opposition wants that situation to prevail.
Similarly, I do not think that the Common"-' wealth meat inspectors, apart from maybe a fewi i individuals, will be found to be to blame. The system I outlined a while ago indicates to me that where the Department of Primary Industry inspectors have been exercising a role, it is quite probable that in every instance they have been carrying out their duties 100 per cent. It has been when they were not with the meat or the times when meat was being transferred that substitution occurred. The procedures for inspection by Department of Primary Industry meat inspectors are very rigorous indeed. The inspectors are very well trained. They themselves, of course, have been pushing for a single meat inspection a.uthority. They want that so that can they can get uniform inspection standards. I must say that when they protest against some procedures they are told they are engaging in industrial disruption. If they carry out their duties to the letter of the regulation-for example, checking out when Commonwealth meat inspectors ask them to lift lids and check whether it is the meat they say it is-they have been accused of industrial disruption. I do not want any of those allegations booted around. In all cases, to my knowledge and from what I have been assured, the Commonwealth meat inspection service has been doing a good job and has been enforcing rigorous meat inspection standards.
Until we can get a single meat inspection authority or delegate authority to the States so that there is only one meat inspection service following the same standards we ,will have trouble with this industry. Until we can get uniform State health laws or national consumer standards we will have problems with this industry. Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

the royal commission the focus is now on the States. The States for the first time have been forced to look at their own systems of inspection, their own control, or lack of control, of kangaroo and horse slaughter and their very patchy system of controls when it comes to game meat, pet food and knackeries. They have been forced to look at the differing arrangements between the States. The States were very critical of how the Federal penalties had lagged behind inflation. One of the major points about the legislation is that it lifts the penalties to $100,000. But when the States criticised us they had even lower penalties for similar forms of abuse.
In the domestic industry, in Victoria within the last two weeks an abuse of the type discovered in the handling of export meat attracted a fine of only $100. I hope that the Victorian Government and all other State governments are really looking urgently at their lack of procedures in inspection, licensing standards and imposing penalties, and will bring their procedures up to date. After all they were so critical of us. The introduction of this legislation has shown how promptly the Federal Government 'flas acted. I believe that it is being shown that far more domestic meat, which is completely outside the control of the Government but completely within the control of the various States, is being adulterated on a scale unheard of at the export level. That perhaps explains why the States are so reluctant to give the full support to our royal commission that they should. I understand, and I would be pleased to be corrected by the Minister if I am wrong, that only one StateVictoria-has so far given its full support for the royal commission to look into all the necessary aspects of the domestic meat industry in Australia as well as the export meat industry.
It is one thing for a State to say that it will agree to the royal commission; it is another thing for it to say that it will give the necessary co-operation so that that royal commission can investigate that section of the meat industry controlled by it. How absurd were some of the statements of the States. They said that they would do something themselves to control their own exports, or that their own exports were fine because of their own standards, but they have no say at all in the export standards of this country-they are completely the responsibility of the Commonwealth-and, if they tried to export; their word would not be ac~ cepted because it is the word of the Australian Government, through the Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation, the Bureau of Animal Health and so on, that is necessary for any inter- ''ltional trading of our meat to take place.

Another point the States have to realise, if they are sincere about doing their best to guarantee that there will never be a repetition of this problem at the export level, is that the only way to guarantee that there is no possibility of the substitution of that type of meat, which was domestic originally, into the export system is for them to put in order their own houses, because obviously there cannot be a problem at the export level if there is no adulterated meat at the domestic level. I plead with the States-in fact I challenge them-to participate fully so that there can be a frank, open and complete royal commission, because we have a tremendous opportunity to clean up the industry for many years to come. Are the States frightened to reveal, through the royal commission, that their standards for domestic meat killing are lower than the export standards or that, in the case of certain types of pet food or knackeries, there is no standard at all so that there is greater substitution of meat at that level because there are no uniform standards between the States? Are they frightened to have a full and frank investigation of their own backyards rather than continually pointing the finger at the Commonwealth? I appeal to them to participate fully and not in a half-hearted manner, as is the case with all States except Victoria at present.
I want to comment now on the royal commission itself. The Minister and the Government have been criticised for not initiating a royal commission at an earlier date. This shows the ignorance of those who are critical of the very delicate international situation in relation to our meat which existed in the early days. Had the Minister or the Government established a royal commission before the United States Department of Agriculture agreed to the assurance of a new procedure so that meat could flow again, it is very likely that pressures within the United States would have prevented the United States Government opening its doors again to Australian meat or allowing meat that was then blocked in the United States from being released onto the market until such time as the royal commission had concluded; and we all know how long royal commissions take before they are finalised. What a tragedy that would have been. So I believe some of the Opposition spokesmen and some of the farmers organisations were rather naive in their calls at that time for a royal commission.
I would say that there are two reasons for establishing a royal commission. One is so that justice can appear to the public generally to be done as well as it is being done. I believe this legislation provides the machinery for justice to be done and for all allegations to be aired. The second reason

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1407

for establishing a royal commission is to highlight, I hope, the relationship between the domestic market and the export market and to recommend better procedures between the two systems and at the State level so that the meat industry will be stronger in the future. Although the meat inspection argument is not a central part of all this, I hope that out of it will come a single federal meat inspection authority, because I believe that is what the industry generally wants and needs.
Let us look quickly at some of the allegations. The meat industry has a long history of allegations, because it is that sort of industry. These allegations were made when Labor was in power and, I am sure, before Labor was in power, just as they have been made since. Most members who represent rural electorates have heard plenty of these allegations, but when it comes to the point of our saying, 'Give me enough information so that I can take it further and have it investigated either by the Minister through the Department or by the police', it is a different matter altogether. That has always been the problem, not only with allegations about the meat industry but also with allegations about other industries.
When we look at the allegations made by the Australasian Meat Industry Employees Union we can see how absurd they were. On the one hand, the union was saying that employment opportunities for its members were declining because of the shortfall of livestock and cattle numbers in this country. Yet its own wild allegations were actually putting at risk the employment of its members because of the reaction they were having on overseas markets. I hope that the union representatives follow through with their allegations so that we can see what substance there is in them. If there have been things wrong with the industry, surely the meat processors would have known of them just as much as the union people knew of them. Rather than making their criticisms now, where was the substantiated information that would have allowed action to be taken earlier? It is one thing for these people, who obviously must have been in the know to a certain extent, to make allegations against everybody else once something happens, but if they did know anything about this matter they should have given solid evidence earlier and avoided the situation of which they were partly the cause.
I go on to the Cattlemen's Union of Australia. If anything has created a scare overseas and put at risk the interests of the members of the Cattlemen's Union-that is, part of the Queensland cattle industry; the killing season is in progress at present-it must have been some of the wild allegations by members of the

Cattlemen's Union. The only justification for some of the things that they were saying seems to be that they like being in front of a television camera. What about the meat inspectors? They support very strongly a single meat inspection system. One has to accept that part of their argument is very sound, but one result of that might be to give them a stronger association. Meat inspectors over the years have been the subject of many allegations concerning free meat and downgrading unless certain things were done. I hope that they respond fully and clean up their sector of the industry for the future.
One other aspect I hope the royal commission will look at is a rumour current in Victoria that somebody in the United States tipped off some of these people in July ahead of the official confirmation to the Australian Government and the Minister on about 11 August. I hope also.that the Commissioner of the Australian Federal Police, Sir Colin Woods, following the comments he made, will make sure that the Federal Police completely clears itself of any suspicion and that, through some very clever police work, will make sure that all those involved in the present scandal are apprehended. Mr PORTER (Barker) (S.28)-Like other members who have spoken in this debate-and I am sure that others who have not spoken share my view-I am disgusted that people could have the gall to export kangaroo meat or horse meat from this country and fraudulently pretend that it was beef. As if that was not bad enough in itself, to do it in the course of our international trade with one of our greatest trading partners, indeed, one of our greatest allies, is an affront to every right thinking Australian. As soon as this racket was uncovered this Government, through the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon), took action. I shall deal with that action in a moment.
I think the matter ought first be put into perspective. The size of the problem is all important. Meat bearing the markings of two of Australia's 400 licensed export establishments has been found to have been adulterated. Both of those establishments are based in Melbourne. Their total exports to the United States so far this year have amounted to some 632 tonnes or 0.39 per cent of

total Australian shipments to the United States over the same period, which amount to 161,000 tonnes. Those figures are one reason for the logical and reasonable approach which has been taken by this Government rather then the hysterical approach which some people have advocated. Perhaps that latter approach was suggested more for political purposes than really to assist the producers and exporters of this country. Clearly, if some of the actions which were demanded of the Government had been taken in the initial stages of this scandal there would still be hundreds of people out of work and probably an enormous amount of meat still tied up in the United States. I believe the Minister acted properly.
For the record I will recite the events as they occurred which led to the introduction of this legislation which increases the penalties applicable to people who fraudulently export meat, pretending that meat to be of one type, beef-in this case when it is not. On 11 August this year the Australian Bureau of Animal Health first received advice from the United States Department of Agriculture that horse meat was suspected to be in a carton of boneless beef bearing the markings 140C which I understand is an Australian meat export establishment in Richmond, Melbourne which trades under the name of Profreeze. On the next day, 12 August, the movement of all export meat from Profreeze was terminated. Seven other boning rooms in the same complex were also placed under strict surveillance and thorough checks were made on their product before it was released for export. On 13 August we were advised that the laboratory tests in the United States proved the meat to be horse meat. Immediately the 140C works were deregisteredo This meant that that company could no longer export meat anywhere in the world. The Australian Federal Police were asked to investi- . gate the matter.
A number of other steps were also taken. Some 1,809 cartons of meat in Victoria bearing the 140C markings, which, if they contained beef, would have had the value of around $100,000, were seized and impdunded for testing. Spot checks were introduced ;in all States. Extra meat inspection surveillance was provided at independent boning rooms and cold stores. Ship loading security was increased. Checking of health certificates and all documentation was intensified. Over the next few days-that was still in August, only a few days after the scandal first became known to the Australian Government-a new system was devised for security seals to be placed on cartons. That is estimated to cost about $1 m

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1411

this financial year. I am advised that the first delivery of those seals was expected on 8 September. An urgent review of the relevant laws was also undertaken at that time. As a result of that review we are now debating these Bills. Quite frankly, when honourable members think about it, it has taken less than a month to get a review under way, and to get legislation drafted, introduced and debated in this House. It must be one of the shortest time frames in which legislation has been initiated and presented to this House. At the same time a thorough police investigation was undertaken and a special task force was set up within the Bureau of Animal Health to co-ordinate a nationwide sampling and species testing program. Following investigations, on 1 September the Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation suspended the export licence of Samsons Meat Co (Vic) Pty Ltd, a company registered in Victoria. Investigations into other companies are continuing.
On 4 September the United States Department of Agriculture confirmed that horse meat had been found in a consignment of meat from establishment 622. I am advised that that is Jason Meats Pty Ltd, Abbotsford, Melbourne. That establishment was immediately deregistered and the Australian Federal Police advised. All cartons bearing the label 622 were seized and tested. Because of that development the Minister took special action in regard to Victoria. He suspended the export operations of 27 independent boning rooms in that State. On 7 September the Minister strengthened the controls by extending the suspension to include independent boning rooms in Victoria that were associated with an abattoir complex. This move involved six boning rooms. The suspension became effective from midnight on 7 September. It will remain in force until the boning rooms can demonstrate adequate security measures and satisfactory species testing programs. I understand that some of those boning rooms have done that already.
New controls are also being implemented whereby export meat will be stored in specially secured and approved cold stores under new inventory control measures. Owners of registered export establishments will be responsible for all activities conducted on their premises. Meat transport vehicles and containers will be sealed to add to the security of the packed product during transport from a boning room to an approved se. cure cold store or ship site. Other measures such as one piece packing containers to make relidding impossible are also under consideration. Further testing procedures, including ongoing sampling systems, are also under consideration. These new

measures are estimated to cost some $6m in this financial year.
Finally, on 8 September, as the Parliament knows, it was announced that there would be a royal commission of inquiry into the meat racket covering, amongst other things, the allegations of malpractice.
That record is not a record of inaction, as has been suggested by our opponents and by others outside this House. It is a record of decisive action taken by the Minister, not in an hysterical framework, but rather by a controlled regulation of the export industry step by step as required.
The four Bills which we are debating cognately increase the penalties in relation to the unlawful exportation of meat. They substantially increase the penalties which are applicable. I think we all agree that it would have been better if those penalties had been increased some time ago. Perhaps one lesson we have learnt out of this is that all of our penalties, not just in this instance, ought to be subject to review.
One thing that concerns me is the call by both Opposition members who have spoken in this debate for retrospective legislation in this case. Whilst I am appalled that this meat substitution racket has taken place, whilst it could have threatened our future meat sales and whilst every member of the Australian community, I am sure, agrees that it was a disastrous situation for the meat industry, the Government has made, I believe, the right decision in not implementing these new penalties retrospectively. The honourable member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin) said that he would like the legislation to be applied retrospectively 'if it would stick'-I think they are the words he used. That attitude is contrary to the natural justice. I believe it is unworthy of any honourable member of parliament to suggest that in this place. That is the case not only for retrospective legislation generally but especially in relation to retrospectivity for criminal laws. I think all honourable members know that people are expected to know the law. One cannot know the law or be expected to know the law if it is changed after the event. Whilst I appreciate the view of almost all Australians that people involved in the meat racket ought to be dealt with severely, that does not provide the basis for us to set a precedent in enacting retrospective legislation in the criminal field. Such an attitude rather reminds me of a lynch gang.
Mr Kerin-A Phil Lynch gang.
Mr PORTER-No, I am not referring to the Minister for Industry and Commerce. But one

1412 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

knows that a lynch gang, incensed by some misdeed, takes the law into its own hands and imposes a penalty on a person who mayor may not be the actual culprit. I, therefore, welcome the Government's decision to impose new penalties from the date of the announcement rather than retrospectively.
Finally, I refer to the meat inspection services generally. For some time, the Federal Government has been endeavouring to resolve a problem of dual inspection of meat. This Government has been acutely aware of the cost to the Federal and State governments, producers and the meat exporters of dual inspections. To clarify the point, as I understand it, presently in some States such as Queensland, export meat is inspected by the Commonwealth meat inspectors and, in some cases, at the same time or at about the same time, it is inspected by State inspectors. Recently the Government implemented an inquiry into this dual inspection service. The report of that inquiry became known as the Kelly report. That report waS presented to Parliament in February 1980. Since that time the Government has been trying to overcome the stupidity of the dual meat inspection. South Australia agreed some years ago to allow the Federal meat inspectors to do the job. The Northern Territory and, more recently, Tasmania have agreed to do likewise. I believe that it is farcical that producers, exporters and the public in New South Wales, Victoria, Queensland and Western Australia have to bear the cost of dual inspection when South Australia, the Northern Territory and Tasmania have apparently seen the light. It is high time that the Commonwealth's initiatives to reduce costs to the people in Queensland, New South Wales, Victoria and Western Australia were taken up by those States. The producers, in particular, have been paying too much for too long. People in those States ought to be asking their State governments why they have to pay for these wasteful dual inspections.
Dual inspections are a disgrace. They are costing beef producers money. They have enough costs to bear now without these wasteful and farcical charges. I support the Minister in his attempts to obtain the agreement of the reluctant States to overcome this stupidity. I support these Bills, as, I believe, does the Opposition because they increase penalties to ensure that the meat scandal is not repeated in Australia in the future.

I guess they were reasonably prompt considering that the initiative came from a report from the American authorities. However, it was only after the Australian Labor Party Opposition and the Australian Democrats had indicated their intention of requiring a full inquiry that the Minister climbed down from his first reaction which was that it was not necessary. I must say that the cattle interests that I have spoken to in my electorate, which is the cattle export centre of Australia, have not been at all impressed with the promptness of that response. I will enlarge a little on the matter of the Kelly Committee. It is well laid out in the Sydney Bulletin of 22 September that this Committee, appointed by the Government, was chaired by Mr Bert Kelly, a one time Liberal member of the House of Representatives and Minister but better known these days under his economist pseudonym as The Modest Farmer'. The Kelly Committee was set up to examine inspection systems and to report to the Minister for Primary Industry. In the Bulletin of 4 August 1981, referring to an answer the Minister for Primary Industry gave in the Parliament when asked about the Kelly report, Mr Kelly wrote: 'I don't think he always catches on as quickly as he could'. I do not think Mr Kelly was being hysterical. Page 41 of the Kelly report states inter ~lia:
. . . at several works visited, works management held the interstate branding stamps. The stamp was applied by an employee and the works merely paid the (inspection) fee.
There was no re-inspection. The committee was told also that the 'Australian Approved' stamp was provided by works management and that if a new stamp was required the inspectors obtained it from management. The Committee said that this practice should cease. I think that there has been

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1413

more than enough notice of that obviously glaring anomaly in what is supposed to be an inspection system. I am not blaming the inspectors. There is one aspect of the meat industry which should be tackled with firm resolution . . . and that is the problem of some managers trying to buy the co-operation of inspectors by giving them meat at ridiculously low prices, or of counternancing excessive overtime claims, or of meat inspectors demanding cheap meat backed by the implied threat of slowing the killing chain or forcing higher offal or other condemnations . . . legislation should include heavy punishment for administrative, VOs (veterinary officers), meat inspectors and management who try to obtain improper advantages . . . in this manner.

It is gratifying to see these meat Bills coming forward. The Opposition supports the strengthening of penalties. I do not accept the misgivings of the honourable member for Barker about retrospectivity in criminal law being a sort of lynch law. It is not of that kind at all it is merely a matter of increasing the penalties to something commensurate with the enormity of the crime. I believe that if it is true, as alleged in some reports, that this is a multi-million dollar fraud then retrospectivity is very well justified in the matter of upgrading the monetary penalties. I can agree in principle that increasing greatly gaol penalties may be an unfair sort of retrospectivity. But certainly I do not object to an increase in monetary penalties, which have existed for many years, without taking any account of inflation, the growth of the industry, the value of it and so on.
The meat industry is worth something like $600m in exports to the United States of America alone. The amount fluctuates enormously. It is subject to many problems other than those of assuring standards. It is subject to the enormous unpredictability that arises from the fact that roughly only 6 per cent of the world's production of beef is ever exported from one country to another. Theoretically it needs only a 6 per cent rise in the domestic production in countries that would otherwise be importing for the importation to cease altogether, and this was the major cause of the disastrous beef slump in the early 1970s.
There is a need for the Government to take note of many of these problems and to act a lot more quickly and promptly than it has in the matter of carcass classification, and then to move quickly to the question of consumer classification -classification at the retail level. This can be done, and it would be a major influence in stabilising the home market. Carcass classification is a major step in standardising prices to growers. The Government and the Department have been dilatory in pursuing this very necessary step forward in a rather vital industry. It is about the third of

1414 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

our primary export industries, and primary exports are still the major factor in our balance of payments.
Home consumption inspection is one of the areas where obviously the Federal philosophy of the Government breaks down. The Government has this phobia about centralism to such an extent that whenever one talks about standardising something on a national scale it has uneasy feelings. Objections are raised in the States and in this Federal Parliament that something called States' rights are being undermined, as though the States were more important than the functions they are supposed to perform and the people they are supposed to serve. It is obvious and clear that there are reasons for having a Federal Government, and a lot of those reasons were known as long ago as the time when our Constitution was formulated. One of those functions is the control of international trade. How in the name of fortune it can make sense to turn back the clock to the days when this continent was occupied by six se1fgoverning dominions of the British Parliament is beyond me.
It is true that in some cases the States, because they are closer to the communities and have more detailed contact, are more appropriate levels of administration for certain functions. It is true that local governments are necessary and appropriate for other functions. It is also true that we should have regional administrations, something between the levels of State and local government. However, the fact remains that some functions are best performed and standardised and to an extent centralised, certainly in the co-ordination of scientific and technological standardisation, at a national level. Surely this must apply to matters such as quarantine and meat inspection for export. If the inspection has to be done on a nationwide scale in every State and every export meat works, it makes no sense at all to have it duplicated in the same meat works just because some of the production is for domestic consumption. All the speakers have agreed that it is high time we eliminated this wasteful dual inspection system and that Australians. were given the same high standard of inspection for beef as are the consumers in those countries to which we export.
