Mr MacKENZIE (Calare) (8.0)-1 speak in support of the Meat Export (Penalties) Bill, the Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Bill, the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Bill and the Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Bill which are being debated cognately. I support those Bills

because they represent the culmination of many hard days work on behalf of the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) and other Ministers and departments concerned. There has been a substantial amount of criticism by industry and other people, including much unfounded media criticism of the Minister for Primary Industry and his Department. I will use some of my time tonight to dispel that criticism by going through almost day by day the actions that have been taken and the consequences of those actions.
On 11 August notification was received of a suspicion that there was horse meat in a consignment of boneless beef from Licence No. 140C, Profreeze. This was detected by the United States Department of Agriculture in San Diego. On 12 August, the day following, the movement of export boneless beef from Profreeze was terminated by the Minister. On 13 August, the next day, it was confirmed that the suspicion was correct and that the consignment was adulterated with horse meat. I understand that that was the first case proven although it may not have been the first suspected case. On the very same day that company was deregistered as an exporter and the Australian Federal Police were asked to institute immediate proceedings. On the next day, the 14 August, which I will call day one, the first day after official notification, the Minister took immediate steps to tighten up security for the export of beef. Those steps were outlined in the statement brought down by the Minister on 8 September. They included the seizure of about $100,000 worth of meat in Victoria, the immediate upgrading of spot checks throughout all States, extra meat inspection and surveillance, an increase in ship loading security and an increased checking of health certifications and documentations.
Within the following days further measures were taken. A new system was drawn up for the security sealing of cartons, a system that may well cost up to $lm in the current financial year. The first delivery of those seals was arranged last week. At the same time, an urgent review of relevant laws was instigat~d. Officers of the Department of Primary Industry, the Attorney General's Department and the Department of Business and Consumer Affairs were involved. Police investigations were further intensified and at one stage I believe that upwards of 18 Australian Federal Police officers were involved with support from the Victoria Police and officers from the Trade Practices Commission. This all took place on day three or four after the official notification that meat substitution had occurred.

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1415

It is very difficult for me to accept criticism of the Minister that nothing was done in those early days. Only one relatively small consignment from one of the 400 registered exporters was affected but perhaps there could be some criticism that in the light of what has subsequently happened we did not take the matter as seriously as would be expected. However, I think that was natural. Nevertheless, on day four the Minister had lengthy discussions with the United States Ambassador and the United States Special Trade Representative. On day seven, 20 August, the Trade Practices Commission was called in to investigate the labelling aspect. On 21 August, on day eight, a new development occurred; kangaroo meat was found in cartons in Melbourne. It was on that day that the suggestion of increasing the penalties for unlawful meat export from $1,000 to $100,000 was first mooted. Many people have forgotten that. Perhaps they do not read their newspapers well enough. The rural Press at least reported at that time the suggestion that such increases in penalties should be invoked.
The next day, following the suggestion that kangaroo meat was involved, the Government apparently arranged for the forwarding of kangaroo serum to the United States to assist in its species testing program. Immediately following that the United States insisted that certain species testing programs be instituted. We agreed in principle. On I September Samsons Meat Co. (Victoria) Pty Ltd was deregistered. I also point out that prior to that, on 27 August, the Minister released a reasonably substantial resume of the action taken so far. I have it with me. It consists of four pages of detailed statements. At all stages the Minister has kept the public and the Parliament very well informed as to the actions taken and, to a lesser degree, the actions proposed. Perhaps he has been too open. This has given the media the opportunity to sensationalise the whole issue and, in many cases, to pick up completely unsubstantiated allegations by various people which are very difficult to refute. At one stage I felt that anybody could ring up the media with the wildest sorts of allegations about goanna meat or whatever and it would be immediately run as up-front headline news.
I refer to the record again. On 4 September the United States Department of Agriculture detected horse meat in another consignment, registered No. 622 from Jason Meats Pty Ltd. Immediately, I understand within hours of confirmation of that detection, the company was deregistered and its stocks impounded. On the same day the operations of 27 independent boning rooms in Victoria were suspended. At that stage

there was some criticism that the Minister's actions were too precipitous and severe but the proof of the pudding was that on the following day the United States Department of Agriculture saw fit to release all meat withheld in the United States except that originating from Victoria. That is indicative of the very good relations with the United States as a customer that the Minister and his Department have been able to maintain throughout the whole difficult business. That has been subsequently proven by the United States Department of Agriculture and the United States authorities working in such close co-operation with the Minister and his Department. They have had the greatest confidence in what the Minister has been doing and have taken very quick action following the actions taken in Australia.
On 6 or 7 September an interim report was handed down from the Australian Federal Police. Legislation for increased penalties under various Acts was in the process of being drafted. I imagine that at that stage there was discussion about setting up a royal commission and that approaches were made to find out who might be available to head that royal commission. Again, the United States had to be kept informed about what was going on. I can well imagine that many people in the United States would not even know what a royal commission is. They probably had substantial fears as to what was implied by a royal commission and whether it would assist or even hinder the processes of resolving this difficulty.
On 7 September the Acting Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) notified the Premiers of the Government's intention to set up a royal commission and sought their co-operation in setting up commissions as far as each State was concerned. The following day we had a statement from the Minister. The day after that we had a statement from the Acting Prime Minister setting out the terms of reference for the royal commission. I would point out that when that very comprehensive statement was introduced into this Parliament by the Minister it was exactly 19 working days from the first day on which the original substantiated notification of horse meat adulteration in the United States was received in Australia. I think that really puts to rest any criticism that has been abroad that the Government did not act with sufficient intentiqn, alacrity or determination in this whole business. On the day following the announcement of the terms of reference for the royal commission the United States announced the resumption of meat imports from Australia. That again, to me, only confirms the very good relationships that the Minister and his Department have had with our customers in the United States

I close on the subject of meat inspection because critics of the Minister and the Government have said that this whole episode has occurred as a result of the dual meat inspection service. That is not correct. In fact there has been no legitimate criticism of the procedures of the Commonwealth meat inspection service in this whole scandal. The regulations and by-laws associated with the handling of meat between certain processing stages prior to export have been faulty. The Commonwealth meat inspection service is an essential component of our export licensing and of our export to the countries that require our meat. It is quite pointless to say, as some Premiers-notably the New South Wales Premier-have, that they will go it on their own. No major importing country will be interested in dealing with meat from New South Wales as against Queensland, Tasmania, or any other State which exports meat. The sensible thing would be for the various States not only to co-operate with the royal commission but also to face the reality that a single meat inspection service is long overdue and in fact will not lead to major unemployment within the industry. The sooner they come to grips with this issue, the better.

Mr CUNNINGHAM (McMillan) (8.21)-1 too support the Meat Export (Penalties) Bill and cognate Bills, which could be described as ones which shut the door after the horse has bolted. Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry) (8.34)-in reply-I would like to thank those who have made sensible contributions to the debate, of which there have been quite a number tonight. There have also been some quite sensible comments from the other side of the House, and I recognise the sincerity with which some views have been put forward. I think the honourable member for McMillan (Mr Cunningham), who preceded me in the debate, has been reading only part of the Press because he claims that the guilty parties in this case will get away with a fine of $100. Does he not realise that
the person recently arrested was charged under the Crimes Act with offences that carry penalties of up to 10 years imprisonment? He has not been charged with an offence that carries a $100 fine. The offences under the Crimes Act with which he has been charged carry twice the level of punishment that can be imposed under the Bills presently before the House. So there will be no chance of anybody getting away with a fine of $100. The honourable member for McMillan ought to do his homework a little better.
Mr Robert Brown-He has to be convicted yet.
Mr NIXON-Of course he has to be convicted-absolutely. But he is charged under the Crimes Act. He is not charged under the legislation we are dealing with tonight with an offence that carries a $100 fine. That is where the honourable member for McMillan has displayed total ignorance. He also raised the question of Senator Primmer. The worst feature of the Senator Primmer allegations is that Senator Primmer refused to name his source, and he still refuses to name his source. He is prepared to cast wide allegations about people but refuses to name the source of the information so that any investigation that takes place can go to the source of the information. He said publicly that he would not name his source. As a senator in another place he has a responsibility to answer to, but he has failed to do so. Senator Primmer does not come out of this smelling like roses by any means for the part he has played. The honourable member for McMillan referred, as did the honourable member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin) and the honourable member for Capricornia (Dr Everingham), to the report of the Committee of Inquiry to Examine Commonwealth and State Meat Inspection Systems~the Kelly report-a copy of which I have brought with me. On page 41 of that report it is stated:

1420 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

It was observed that at several works visited, works management held the interstate branding stamps. The stamp was applied by an employee and the works merely paid the fee. Iii other words, no re-inspection occurred.
That was the first complaint in the Kelly report. The fact is that that has nothing to do with the Department of Primary Industry or the Minister for Primary Industry. It is totally a State matter. The Kelly report was tabled in this Parliament and sent to the States for them to study and to take action on the matters that related to them. So to charge the Department of Primary Industry or the Federal Minister for Primary Industry with lack of action in that case is just simple nonsense and shows a complete lack of understanding by the honourable member for Werriwa, the honourable member for McMillan and the honourable member for Capricornia. The second charge was:
We were also told that, in many if not all cases, the 'Australia Approved' stamp was provided by works management, and iF a new stamp was required, the inspectors obtained it from management. This practice should cease.
It is extraordinary how, with hindsight, everybody is saying how brilliant a deduction that was. In point of fact it is common practice in exporting countries around the world for management to hold the stamp, to control the stamp and to have it replaced in that way. But with some sense of prescience the Kelly Committee was able to see trouble in that, and I give the Committee full marks'for doing so. But that is only one part of the problem. The fact is that the method used by the Australian export industry was approved by the United States Department of Agriculture, the Federal Republic of Germany, the Japanese Government and all the importing countries. Moves were taken, following the publishing of the Kelly report, for the system of control of the stamp to be structurally tightened up. However, that is not where the problem lay either in terms of the whole of the meat scandal. The fact is, as one speaker properly pointed out, that the Commonwealth inspection service was not criticised for, or at fault in, its inspection of the meat. It was during the transfer of the meat from the boning room and the packing room to the cold stores, within the cold stores and during its removal from there to the ship that the substitution took place. It is there that we have been at great pains to tighten the regulations and control so that we can satisfy the importing countries that we have this matter completely under control.
The honourable member for Murray (Mr Lloyd), the honourable member for Barker (Mr Porter) and the honourable member for Calare (MacKenzie) outlined the actions that have been taken since the first announcement on 11 August and, I thought, spoke quite accurately about the

procedures that were necessary to get this matter back in order. I think the honourable member for Calare pointed out that the whole range of actions occurred within 19 days.
Several things were necessary upon discovery of this matter. It was necessary to get in the police and to have them investigate thoroughly as far as they could go. That was essential. It was necessary to get the meat that was locked up in America out of the lock up and back on to the market place. That was done. It was very necessary to get procedures in Australia that would satisfy the importing countries that this event could not occur again. That was a vital necessity for the future of the meat industry. That was also don.e. It was necessary to get procedures for testing ot the meat types in a way that would be approved by the importing countries. That has been done.
The real test of this matter is what price is being obtained for the meat in America today. It is with some pride that I noticed, indeed despite the difficulties we have been through, that the price for lean cow beef in America today is in fact higher than it was prior to 11 August when the first substitution was found. That is the real test. There is no better test than that. I am grateful to the American Administration for its co-operation in the whole of this matter. There was close cooperation, indeed hourly and daily telephone calls on exchanges of information through the period, to ensure that we were on a common track.
I turn now to the royal commission. A great number of allegations have been made. Many of them were hairy and wild; nevertheless, they were made. There has been good media coverage of the allegations over the past two or three weeks. The purpose of the royal commission is to give those who have made the allegations an opportunity to go before the commission to place before it evidence that they believe they have and that they have stated publicly in respect of these allegations. That will be the real test of a great number of people around this country. I will follow with some interest, for example, what Senator Primmer, who has made allegations, does when he has the protection. of the royal commission. He had the protection of the: Parliament but he was not prepared to use it so that proper evidence could be given. Let us see what he does with the royal commission. A number of other people have made allegations and have claimed that they have information and have known about these sorts of things for a long time. I will be interested to see how they handle themselves before the royal commission. One of these days, after the royal commission has finished its inquiries, I will come into

Meat Export (Penalties) Bill

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1421

the Parliament to make another speech on the allegations.
Another matter that has raised a great deal of interest is the prospect of getting a single meat inspection service. The Kelly report sought a single meat inspection service and it then gave us all of the-reasons it would be too hard to get. Maybe it was because of the presentation of the Kelly report that the States had some difficulty in accepting what was written in it. They were critical of the report-they still are critical of it-the substance of it, and some of the comments in it. Nevertheless, ever since the Kelly report was brought down we have battled away with the States in trying to get a single meat inspection service. Fortunately Victoria has agreed to co~ operate in a study to obtain a service in Victoria. If we can get Victoria across the barrier on this matter we might be able to spread the service into other States. The most intransigent States have been Queensland, New South Wales and Western Australia. I think it is on the eastern seaboard that the greatest need for a single meat inspection service can be seen. That is where the major transfers of meat have taken place and where standards of inspection vary-they are almost non-existent in some parts-in respect of pet food and domestic meat supplies. This problem certainly needs to be overcome. Somebody raised the following question here tonight: Why should the people of a State in Australia eat meat from an abattoir with a lesser inspection service than that demanded by importing countries? I think there is a fair bit of validity in that. '
A further point I would like to make is that the royal cOIhmission we have announced affects only export meat. Of course, we do not have the power to look at the domestic meat market unless the States make a reference. I have read in the Press that Victoria has been seeking to widen the terms of reference of the royal commission to look at domestic meat. But the fact is that all Victoria needed to do was to give the royal commission a term of reference. I notice that it has now done that. In fact, it has mirrored our terms of reference so that the royal commissioner can look at export meat and, under similar terms of reference, at domestic meat in Victoria.
Sympathetic noises are coming out of New South Wales but I do not think we have yet had anything concrete from the Premier of New South Wales on the matter. Sympathetic noises are coming out of Queensland about co-operating with the royal commission but I do not know whether we have yet had anything concrete out of Queensland either. But for this meat industry to

be finally cleaned up to the satisfaction of everybody concerned it is necessary and desirable that the other States give a reference to the royal commission so that people can be subpoenaed and information on the whole of the meat industry can be given to the commission. Only in that way will we know at the end of the day that we have a totally satisfactory meat industry throughout Australia.
This has been perhaps the most unfortunate episode to occur to a rural industry in the last 30 or 40 years. I cannot recall anything quite so devastating. The impact has been enormous and the bad publicity that Australia has gained from it has been enormous. Only two meat works out of 400 in the whole of Australia were involved, a miserable percentage of the total meat works, but enormous damage has been wrought on the industry. I only hope, now that we have the new procedures in place, now that the meat is moving again and the royal commissioner is looking at the allegations, we can look forward to Australia reestablishing itself as having one of the best and purest meat industries in the world.
Question resolved in the affirmative.

Will he also take immediate steps to ensure that no contractual arrangements are entered into until this inquiry is concluded , , .

Recent finds of diamonds in north-west Australia
indicate one of the largest diamond fields in the world. Recently the Melbourne Age featured
facts which have been concealed by those involved in the process of a complete takeover. The
reply of the Deputy Prime Minister was simply this:

It is basically the organisation's responsibility to its share-
holders to sell its products in a manner which is in the best interests of those shareholders.

I must confess that I do not necessarily disagree

with that, so far as the firm is concerned. But min-

simple market dictum. Strange as it may seem, the

same Deputy Prime Minister insisted on being in

Japan for the renegotiation of iron ore contracts

and the same Deputy Prime Minister insisted on

being in Japan to renegotiate coal contracts. He

claimed that Australia's raw materials should not

The recommendations read as follows-

12. RECOMMENDATIONS

It is recommended that the following steps be taken to encourage the further processing of gemstones within Australia:

12.1 Tax averaging provisions currently applicable to primary producers be examined with a view of their application to ge,mstone miners.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1423

12.2 The term "processed gemstones" used by the Export Development Grants Board to determine eligibility for the export assistance schemes be carefully defined to include only, "processing to a form ready for mounting in jewellery".
I2.3 The cost of training cutters be eligible for assistance under training schemes such as NEAT or SYETP for the whole period of training.
12.4 Approval and possibly assistance be given to the importing of skilled overseas cutters for limited periods to allow the in-house training of local cutters.
12.5 Technical Colleges and similar institutions be encouraged to set up courses in gemmology and lapidary, which at present is almost solely the function of amateur clubs and societies.
12.6 Gemstone marketing facilities be investigated with a view to encourage the orderly development of the industry by assisting miners to recover high prices for gemstones from increased competition, and by assisting local processors to secure access to rough stone.
12.7 In negotiations for the establishment of a diamond mining industry in Western Australia, Government has regard to the benefits that would arise from the establishment of a diamond processing industry.
12.8 Government grants or subsidies be made available for research int') the improvement of Australian sapphires by heat or other treatment, and into the cutting, polishing and marketing of gemstones. These funds could be made available, on a dollar for dollar basis, for approved projects.
12.9 Investigate the removal of sales tax on the equipment used in processing, and on all gemstones cut and processed in Australia from rough material of Australian origin.
12.10 Consider the provision of short term low interest rate loans for people and firms expanding or entering the gemstone processing industry.,
Mr JACOBI-I thank the House. Although the report 'Australian Gemstone IndustryPotential for Expansion' gave rise to some expectation of action, not one of the recommendations has been implemented. The industry appears doomed because of this Government's failure to act. No matter what this Government might say, and one can easily imagine its response, the fact remains that it would not treat primary industry in the same manner. A month ago the Deputy Prime Minister attended a business luncheon at the Oberoi hotel in Adelaide in my State. During the question period he was asked a question concerning the gem industry. He replied:
If there is any problem in the gem industry it's news to me.
He then promptly sat down. This is typical of the Government's response to the industry's plea for assistance. It is an indictment of his knowledge of the portfolio that he purports to manage.
Mr Hyde-I do not think you are being charitable.
Mr JACOBI-I am not being charitable but at least I am being honest. Surely the fact of setting up a working party with the intention of the eventual submission of a report specifies an urgent requirement within that industry. Surely serious

consideration of the facts concerning the gem industry, its potential-both nationally and internationally-and the effects of our balance of payments must result in grave doubts being cast on the actions of the Government in its recent move to increase sales tax and to increase interest rates, to the extent of forcing people out of their homes, while at the same time ignoring the Australian gem industry and its enormous potential involving up to $1 billion a year. When compared with other countries, most of which must first import the unprocessed gem material from other parts of the world, the Australian situation is unique and is one of extreme envy. This country should not permit the export of rough or unprocessed gem material at the expense of the Australian economy. If the Government is of the opinion that we must sacrifice our national heritage at the expense of the Australian taxpayer, then the exported rough material should be subject to an export tariff. If so, the Australian gem industry certainly must be consulted.
An industry of the magnitude of the gem industry, apart from its economic potential, will provide employment for many people, both in this country and overseas. The mining area would prosper and large expansion would be possible. The specialised areas of processing can be seen as extensive but only as an extension of the present structure. Dealers both in and out of Australia would handle a wide range of products. Manufacturing would double or triple in size. One of the recommendations contained in the report suggests the removal of sales tax on all gemstones cut and processed in Australia from rough material of Australian origin. This matter has been referred to the Treasurer (Mr Howard) but up to this point no reply has been received. Implementation of the recommendation would result in greater assistance to the industry. Sales within the country would increase and what the Treasury lost in sales tax revenue would be more than compensated for in extra direct tax paid by gem companies as a result of increased turnover.
When the report was prepared and published the prospects for the Australian diamond industry were less well established than they are now. But to some extent the report does deal with diamonds. It makes it clear that in other countries which have diamond industries constructive government action has been effective in protecting and promoting this industry. It has not been left in the hands of private interests, foreign multinationals in South Africa or Israel. It makes clear the need for the Commonwealth and Western Australian governments to take action to ensure that diamonds produced from the Argyle

1424 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

deposit in Western Australia are processed in this country and are marketed in this nation's interests. Only in this way will the full benefit of these minerals accrue to our people.
But this Government and the Western Australian Government have done nothing to secure our national interest in this deposit. They seem happy for Australia to be sold out by the Ashton partners. These companies include CRA which has already been given privileged status as an honorary 'Australianising' company, despite its current overseas control. I refer to its naturalisation policy. The Government should immediately impose export controls to prevent the Ashton partners from selling out Australia's interests. It should then investigate ways of developing a diamond cutting industry in this country. It should examine marketing arrangements to see how Argyle diamonds can be marketed to maximise the return to Australia. Surely the Minister and the Government have had more than sufficient time to act on the recommendations of the report as they affect the gem industry in this country if, in the nation's interest, we are to preserve both industries.
Mr ADERMANN (Fisher) (8.59)-I rise to support the philosophy and the thrust of the Budget, although I have some suggestions for the Government which I will reveal later. I believe the Budget builds on the very sound economic base established by the responsible reconstruction and management of the Fraser-Anthony Government. That is why I have been very disappointed in the quality of the debate that has come from the Opposition benches. There has been nothing constructive; it has all been critical. There has been no remorse. Nothing has been learnt from the disaster of three years of Labor government. It talks about coming back into government. That would be as disastrous as giving an arsonist a box of matches and a can of kerosene.
The Budget is directed at removing some of the upward pressure on interest rates by achieving a commendable domestic surplus which, with other decisions, ensures that Government borrowings at all levels, which necessarily co-exist where deficits have to be financed, can be reduced. The Budget maintains the urgent and necessary curbs on the inflationary pressures which we cannot ignore. It provides a base which, I hope, can well provide
the Government with a greater capacity to look at and to relieve some of the disincentives, inequities and anomalies in our income tax laws. It evidences a very constructive concern for the problems associated with unemployment and a firm resolve to combat unemployment and consolidate the environment which allows the private sector

increasingly to provide more and !)lore jobs and employment opportunities. Those aims and purposes summarise the thrust of the Budget. It is the course which I believe we must maintain.
However, there are some matters in which I have experienced some disappointment. I will persist in bringing them to the Government's notice. I concede that in the International Year of Disabled Persons some very worthwhile concessions and assistance have been given to very many disabled people in very many areas. But, particularly, in this year of the disabled there is a glaring area in which remedial action has been very seriously neglected. Indeed, I hoped that in this Budget that might have been specifically redressed because it is one of the disadvantages and discriminations experienced by one section of the disabled. Unfortunately, all I have had to this date are pious statements and not much action. I refer to letters that I have written to many Ministers, to questions which I have placed on the Notice Paper but which have attracted very disappointing answers and to a speech that I have made in this House in the grievance debate. Sometimes I wonder whether speeches made in this place by back benchers serve any greater purpose that just to fill up Hansard or sometimes a space in the member's electorate newspaper. Whether government or busy Ministers ever take much cognisance of them is a debatable point. Certainly, the Press gallery is always, as we see it tonight, virtually deserted. Tonight one solitary soul there is watching the proceedings. I hope that grievance debates, adjournment debates and those sorts of debates do not just become an opportunity for members to let off a bit of steam without anybody taking them very seriously.
I refer to those matters again because I have raised them. Despite all of that, deaf people, who surely must constitute a big section of the disabled people are still being prevented, because of the prohibitive cost, from purchasing a visual printout television attachment. Why? Because the Government still imposes a savage sales tax on those attachments even when medical certificates of profound deafness. can be produced. I had hoped that something might be done in this Budget rather than increasing the sales tax level. So deaf people are denied print-outs of news, public information, police warnings, weather alerts, et cetera, because the attachments to make this possible are so expensive. The Government, in this year of the handicapped, seems still to be interested only in the miniscule sales tax receipts it can harvest and not in alleviating the sorry disadvantage of the deaf. That, to me, is unforgivable. I have said that before in this House.

Appropriation Bill (No. I)

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1425

Neither has the Government, despite my pleas, in any way done anything to incorporate in its departments, police stations or ambulance stations, one single porta printa, the deaf print-out telephone, so that deaf people can obtain help and assistance by telephone. Neither has Telecom Australia been at all helpful in seeing that such telephones can be made available on a reasonable rental basis for the deaf. So in the year of the disabled a wonderful new telephone which could open the world to the deaf and be a lifeline for them but which costs so much that they cannot afford it-and they cannot rent it-does not evoke any positive response from Telecom or the Government. I say this sadly because Australia compares very unfavourably with other countries such as the United States of America in this matter. There are tens of thousands of deaf people in Australia who are desperately in need of some consideration. Yet we seem to be content to
ensure that they remain imprisoned behind the walls of isolation, silence and loneliness. This Budget does not contain either joy or hope for them. I care little about whether I hurt or offend by making these statements because I want more than pious platitudes about aid to this section of the disabled. All the pleas that I have made in this House have been rejected or ignored. I suppose that I have to keep hammering away and hope that at some time some action will emerge in this field.
I wish to deal with another matter in which a very severe handicap is still being imposed on a disadvantaged section of the community. I turn to the sales tax. Certainly, sales tax is a talking point in this Budget debate. With all of the disadvantages of remoteness, the disadvantages of every-
thing costing more because of transport and service costs, the disadvantage of less than adequate communication and still totally inadequate television and media availability, how we can persist in levying sales tax on freight and service costs on top of the city wholesale cost of goods to people in country areas just cannot be in any way excused or condoned. That is an unforgivable penalty to country people. Sales tax on the freight component of costs has no justifiable basis either in justice or in logic. Yet the honourable member for Dawson (Mr Braithwaite)-he spoke about this
the other night-and the honourable members for Kennedy (Mr Katter), Maranoa (Mr Ian Cameron), Leichhardt (Mr Thomson), Wide Bay (Mr Millar) and I and, I suspect, in earlier days the honourable member for Darling Downs (Mr McVeigh) have pointed out that injustice and have asked for its removal for so long without even a glimmer of response that it seems that we

are just whistling in the wind. We country representatives are resilient fellows. The Government can be assured that although we support fully the thrust and intent of the Budget we will nevertheless continue to belt its ear to endeavour to achieve justice for people in remote areas away from capital cities.
I turn to the Department of Administrative Services. I would like the Minister for Administrative Services (Mr Newman) to take some notice of another matter that I raise. I point out to the Government and to the Minister the completely unsatisfactory and unfair situation pertaining to Queensland federal electorates. In 1977 an amendment was made to the Commonwealth Electoral Act which I felt then, and still feel, was most inequitable and discriminatory to nonmetropolitan electors. That amendment provided that instead of an allowance in the permissible variation of 20 per cent from the ascertained quota, an allowance of 10 per cent would apply. But to make it worse, the actual practical application of that amendment gave even greater cause for concern. That variation, little as it was, was provided by the Parliament to give some adequ~te representation to people in large electorates wIth distant and separate areas of population, often separated by large distances of very sparse population. When a redistribution was undertaken in Queensland in 1977 the quota was determined at 65,877. That should have allowed the huge electorates of Kennedy, .Maranoa, Leichhardt and perhaps Dawson and Wide Bay, in particular, under the words and intent of the Act passed by the Parliament to vary below that figure by up to 6 587 electors. I ask leave to incorporate in Hansard.a table that was compiled by the Australian Electoral Office which shows, in fact, the electoral enrolments of the electorates at that time, 1 November 1977. Mr ADERMANN-The tolerance in the electonite of Kennedy, as we can see, was minus 5.66 per cent; in Maranoa, less than 2 per cent; in Dawson, 1.3 per cent and so on. So much for the 10 per cent allowable variation from quota. That section was inserted in the Act with deliberate intent and purpose. When we study this table can anyone honestly claim that that intent and purpose was either considered or applied appropriately? Only one electorate exceeded 5 per cent. No other electorate came within cooee of even 5 per cent, let alone 10 per cent.
Another intent of the Act is that there should be consideration of future growth trends and community of interests. I will illustrate how, in Queensland, it is extremely difficult or impossible to understand how those precepts were considered by referring to my own electorate of Fisher. The Sunshine Coast area, certainly an

identifiable unit with absolutely homogeneous interests, was split in two, some remaining in the electorate of Fisher and some being placed in the electorate of Wide Bay. So much for the consideration of community of interest. That caused distress and bewilderment to the residents of the Sunshine Coast. But in a further decision which seems to defy all understanding the northern outer metropolitan area of Brisbane was combined with a large portiqn of the Sunshine Coast in the electorate of Fisher. The Sunshine Coast is possibly the fastest growing area in Australia. The new outer metropolitan area is also an area of very rapid expansion and growth. Predictably, by placing those two rapid growth areas in the one electorate there was a complete departure from the principle of consideration of future growth on electoral enrolments.
As anyone could have predicted, enrolments in

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1427

Fisher have increased from 65,835 to nearly 90,000. It is the electorate in Australia with the heaviest enrolment. Both the Sunshine Coast and the North Pine subdivision were very greatly disadvantaged by that decision, which ensured that their combined growth made enrolment numbers almost unmanageable. It makes me seriously wonder what consideration is given to the expressions and wishes of this Parliament and what is the purpose of legislation if its intent is so very clearly departed from in practice when those boundaries are drawn.
That brings me to the situation now existing in Queensland. The State average, which would suffice as the notional quota which would now appertain, has grown to 72,331. I ask leave to incorporate in Hansard a table compiled by the Australian Electoral Office showing details of enrolments in Queensland electorates at 31 July 1981 and the deviation from the State average, together with the areas of those electorates.

Mr ADERMANN-It will be seen that the
electorates of Fisher, Fadden and McPherson were then above that average by 21.9 per cent,
21.23 per cent and 20.79 per cent respectively,
and the difference is even greater now. How such
a situation can be regarded as fair to the electors and to the members representing those massive
populations completely escapes me. In addition,

the table shows that the numbers of electors in the electorates of Griffith, Kennedy and Moretontwo city electorates and one country electorateare 13.25 per cent, 14 per cent and 10.28 per cent below the State average. No less than six of Queensland's 19 electorates vary from the notional quota by greater than 10 per cent and three of them vary by greater than 20 per cent. Thus 30.6 per cent of Queensland's electorates vary by greater than 10 per cent despite what the Act says, 15.3 per cent of them being greater than 20 per cent in variation from the notional quota. All of this has occurred within 4 years, so I do not think I am being unkind, uncharitable or illogical in condemning that redistribution as in virtually every sense unsatisfactory and in contravention of the intent of the Parliament and the Act.
Section 25 (2) (b) of the Act provides that a redistribution of any State into divisions may-I concede that the word is 'may' and not 'must'-be made whenever in one-fourth or 25 per cent of the divisions of the State the number of electors differs from a quota ascertained in accordance with this part to a greater extent than one-tenth more or one-tenth less. I believe the Government will be completely remiss and culpable if it does not now act under that section of the Act. Over 30 per cent of divisions are in that range-three electorates being more than 20 per cent at variance, two being more than 13 per cent and another more than 10 per cent. It is no excuse for the Government to indicate that the Act provides for no absolute compulsion. If this Government inserted that provision in the Act-presumably it meant it to have the intent that it announced the Act would have-then it cannot for any reason now dismiss the situation and let it become worse and worse as it most assuredly will.
When the electorates of Fisher, Fadden and McPherson are much of the time experiencing new enrolments at the rate of 1,000 or even up to 1,500 a month the situation must be quickly and properly rectified. Before the next election, whenever that will be-soon or late-almost certainly at least one additional electorate, namely Lilley, and perhaps one country and up to two city electorates might also vary from the quota by more than 10 per cent. With all the respect and deference that I can muster I cannot see how any responsible government can sit back and allow that situation to develop, nor can I see how the Government can responsibly allow the situation already in existence to continue. The culpability of the Government would be increased if it let that pass because in a most unwise and, I believe, inequitable decision it altered the variation from

1428 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

20 per cent to 10 per cent. I believe very strongly it ought to revert to 20 per cent.
Having established the 10 per cent differential, the Government now ignores the alarming discrepancies in Queensland. I repeat that 30.6 per cent of Queensland electorates are demonstrably more than 10 per .cent out of kilter with the quota. It cannot have it both ways: Either 20 per cent is the fair and reasonable variation figure or, if it is to be 10 per cent, the boundaries of the electorates should be drawn to comply with that; otherwise the section of the Act which spells out the very definite intent of the Parliament-that is, when 25 per cent of divisions are in this category the proper course is to redistribute-becomes just an exercise in semantics and nonsense. If the Government had no such intent in the legislation it ought not to have written the words into the Act because that could be interpreted as a deception.
Furthermore, it should make clear to any distribution commissioners that the percentage of variation from the quota allowed should be used to provide equity and fairness to remote and disadvantaged electors. If it does not, if that section is ignored, the Act becomes a nonsense and is pointless. It is pointless putting any figure into the Act, whether it is 20 per cent, 10 per cent or any other figure, if nobody will take any notice of it. Community of interest has to be stressed to the commissioners as being more than a vague, meaningless string of words. There has to be an insistence by the Government that that principle is observed in the consideration of electoral boundaries.
I believe the Government is duty bound to undertake now a redistribution in the Federal electorates of Queensland because the situation is demonstrably worse and more inequitable and unfair than it was before the last redistribution. It is a situation where it is unreasonable and unfair to electors to be in the situation where enrolments are approaching 90,000 and still rapidly growing and a busy member is expected to try to service such a huge number of electors. It is certainly a recipe for more heart attacks, collapses and ulcers for members who with one office and two staff members are trying to do their level best to represent the causes and aspirations of so many constituents. There is no moral or fair justification for the Government to refuse to rectify that situation because the Act provides for a clear intent. It uses the word 'may'. Even though it does not use the word 'must' the intent is there; it was inserted in the Act.
I would like to know that the matters I have raised tonight will not just pass unnoticed as pages in Hansard. I hope too, that the contributions of

many back bench members in this debate and other debates, some of them excellently thoughtful and constructive, somehow make somdmpact somewhere on the Government and on the Parliament. I believe the matters I have raised tonight are important. I repeat that I fully support the thrust and the purpose of the Budget. I will most certainly continue to support the very sound economic management which has kept inflation and interest rates lower than in almost every other country and which has resulted in a very sig-
nificant expansion in the number of jobs and job opportunities, which again compares most favourably in the world scene. I do not commend the Government, however, in the matters of disappointment I have raised. I believe they cannot be swept under the carpet because they represent injustices that must be redressed. Each of tliem, I believe-the Government ought to note themimpinges on the credibility of the Government, and they are by no means insignificant.
Mr MORRIS (Shortland) (9.19)-This Budget does nothing for Australia's future despite all the enforced hardship and sacrifice of almost six years of Fraserism. Rather, there will be more inflation, higher interest rates, less economic growth, reduced access to health services, no hope for the unemployed and ever-increasing taxes. In short, it continues a savage attack of successive Fraser governments on the general living standards of Australian families. It is a completely inappropriate Budget for the prevailing social and economic conditions in Australia. Let me quote the words used by Mr Eric Risstrom of the Australian Taxpayers Association, a man of impeccable objectivity and credibility, to describe this Government's 1981-82 Budget. Mr Risstrom said that the Budget was the worst he had ~een in 34 years. He said that there was no relief for average wage earners while indirect taxes would rise by $892m. He said the Budget indicated an inflation rate of 13.5 per cent for 1981-82. The Sydney Morning Herald in its editorial of 29 August 1981 said that the Budget, which further strands and penalises those on social services and low incomes, has been seen as indicating the Government's continued lack of a human face.-
This Budget forces th~ poor, the unemployed, the weak, small and medium sized businesses, and small farmers to compete for limited resources, for sparse Government assistance, if any, against high interest rates and the limited credit left after the powerful, substantially overseas-controlled mineral companies have taken their fill. The rich and powerful are feather-bedded while ordinary Australians are denied access to health care and sustenance, forced to pay extortionate interest

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1429

rates on their home loans, and pilloried when they are unable to secure employment.
Let us look briefly at the management of transport responsibilities under this Government. The National Country Party has always sought to monopolise the Federal transport portfolio in order to control transport of primary exports and the allocation of road funds, yet rarely has it demonstrated qualities of national leadership, leaving that task to its unfortunate colleagues in the Liberal Party. Whilst at least the Liberals are forced to address national questions, the National Country Party crudely supports a few powerful sectional interests, with utter disregard for the wider criteria of the national perspective or, indeed, ordinary country people. It goes through the motions of government, but the reality is that the National Country Party has established pork barrelling as a major mode of resource allocation.
Both coalition partners are inextricably linked to sectional interests. The National Country Party's professed concern with small and medium farming interests is as mythological as the Government's notion of private enterprise, which may have had some truth in the past but is now pure ideology aimed at masking the reality of its objectives. The fact is that the National Country Party is switching its allegiances from agricultural exporters to mineral exporters and organising the transport system for their benefit. Road, rail, shipping and port infrastructure and services are being used to subsidise bulk mineral exports. Customers are being forced to cross-subsidise giant mining companies, which face widely fluctuating world prices. In the Government's jargon, the reality of making room for private enterprise to grow in the transport area is the abrogation of national responsibilities by this Government, not just in budgetary appropriation but also in the organisation of transport, leaving the field open to a very small number of giant transport conglomerates to organise transport for their sectional ends.
This Government has deliberately created a vacuum into which large concerns which own or control assets in all transport modes-road, rail, shipping, air and ports-are able to create their own de facto national transport policies. Transport has been handed on a platter to an imperfect market. The system is unco-ordinated, inefficient, wasteful of fuel, and largely organised for the benefit of suppliers of services and a few major customers. Road grants already announced amount to $685m for 1981-82, an increase of 9.1 per cent on 1980-81. I hope the Government and those opposite do not have the audacitYio suggest that this maintains real levels of funding: as they promised for the period from 1977-78 to 1980-81,

because real grants fell over that period, even using the Government's inappropriate cost indices. The levels of increases in funding of 7.2 per cent for 1982-83 and 6 per cent for 1983-84 and 1984-85 will reduce the level of federally funded road works.
The Government's index, the Bureau of Transport Economics road construction price index, lacks accuracy. Almost every other road construction cost index has risen more than this index. The BTE index rose 139.5 per cent between 1968-69 and 1978-79 while that of the New South Wales Department of Main Roads rose 198 per cent over the same period. Until the BTE index is revised, the Government is using the implicit price deflator of gross fixed capital expenditure on private non-dwelling construction to measure road costs, an index which does not even include bitumen prices. Because these indices are national indices they take no account of the differential cost pressures suffered by different levels or localities of government. Clearly, the Government is using contrived statistics to mask the fact that its commitment to road users is falling while road users are forced to pay outrageous costs for fuel, half of which goes to the Treasury in Canberra.
In 1974-75 the Commonwealth funded 32.2 per cent of total road expenditure. By 1978-79, the years or Fraserism, its share had fallen to 27.3 per cent, and the decline continues. In Victoria some councils have been forced to rip up sealed roads because they cannot afford to carry out essential maintenance work. The onus of maintaining the roads network is being pushed onto State and local governments, and if they abrogate their enforced increased responsibilities, as the Commonwealth has done, the road network will deteriorate even further. In New South Wales the Government's expenditure on road funding has risen 24 per cent in real terms over the past four years compared to an 8 per cent cut in Federal road expenditure. One sure result is that State and local government charges will have to rise to fund the areas the Commonwealth has rejected. There is ample evidence to show that the cost to the community of a deteriorating road network far exceeds the cost of road unkeep. The Government is turning its back on an excellent social investment which could save many lives and avoid many tragic road casualties. So if people need good roads to get to work, to health services, or for recreation, this Government has ignored them. Manufacturers or truckies who need good roads to make a profit or a decent income have also been ignored by the Government. As to the

1430 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

farmer who needs good rural roads to sell his produce, his mythical benefactor, the National Country Party, has turned its back on him too. Let me quote from 'Farm Focus: The 80's', which was published by the National Federation in January 1981. It stated:
The importance of an efficient road system to the cost of transporting farm produce is obvious. Considerable upgrading is necessary if the road system is to meet the needs of the 80's satisfactorily .
It continues:
There needs to be substantial increases in Budget appropriations for rural roads during the 80's.
So much for the National Country Party's pretended concern for country interests. For those who do not happen to be among the thousands of small and medium sized domestic manufacturing companies or farmers or just ordinary Australians, but are among the few powerful mining or vested interests, the story is different. They will benefit under this Government's policies.
The problem for this Government in reducing Federal expenditure on roads and capital works is that it has reduced opportunities for the major private sector companies dependent on those works. To assist these companies the Government has now abolished the long-standing discretionary power of the States to determine which national road construction projects are offered to private contractors through tendering procedures. Its objective is to divert road construction works away from public road authorities to the large private concerns which strongly support the Liberal and National Country parties. There is no evidence whatsoever, as the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) admits, that private contractors, who get at least 50 per cent of the work anyway, can build roads cheaper or better than public road authorities such as the departments of main roads in the various States.
On Monday I met a deputation of New South Wales Department of Main Roads employees who were deeply concerned at the prospect of losing their jobs because of this Government's sell-out to sectional interests, the Australian Federation of Construction Contractors. They presented me with a petition, which I will be handing to the Minister for Transport later this week, signed by 211 employees with service ranging up to 35 years with the Department of Main Roads. It outlines their protest at the Fraser Government's ill-considered, ill-conceived action in seeking to divert road works away from the Department of Main Roads to private companies. cent in real terms on last year's figure following the abolition last year of the Labor Government's highly successful Minor Improvements Traffic and Engineering Road Satety Program. From a transport view, the main point to make about this Budget is that it does nothing to create an energy efficient national transport system geared to serve consumers, not suppliers. It creates space for multi-modal giant corporations to organise the transport system, as we have seen with the airlines, so as to maximise benefits for themselves and a few powerful customers. By intracorporate pricing they can encourage use of the services that will yield maximum profits and avoid tax by shipping profits abroad irrespective of the social costs or the national criteria involved.
I make brief mention of the shambles that has now developed in the provision of essential services to the aviation industry. Because of previous inadequate financial provisions for aviation services and unrealistic staff ceilings imposed by the Prime Minister on the Department of Transport, safety levels in air services have been seriously endangered. Brisbane Airport is now being closed down three hours early each day because not enough air traffic controllers are available to operate the airport. Those air traffic controllers in employment are being required to work excessive overtime to maintain services at other airports. There is no doubt that Brisbane Airport is only the first airport to be closed down daily because of staff shortages. The responsibility for this costly, disruptive and ludicrous situation lies with the Minister for Transport. His incompetence and mismanagement are also major factors in the airport closures and the disastrous situation developing in the provision of air services. His ad hoc attempts to recruit key technical personnel and air traffic controllers for his Department will prove futile unless the conditions and entitlements of the officers concerned are brought into line with those available elsewhere.
There needs to be a national perspective in transport. It should be geared to the needs of consumers, not suppliers. These are simple aims but they are not supported by this conservative and reactionary Government. Those aims are worth the effort required to realise them. There needs also to be a wider set of influences on transport services to ensure that they serve a wider set of criteria consistent with a social audit of costs and benefits, democratic participation and greater coordination of services and modes. We could then make a start on a soundly based national transport policy co-ordinated with an energy policy, something for which Australians have waited too long and without which our transport system

will always be costly, inequitable, inadequate and inefficient. I turn now to a matter in my own electorate arising from the Government's arrogant and callous decision to abolish the Community Youth Support Scheme. ears. He did, however, receive support and assurances from the Opposition and its' shadow Minister.

The Government'S callous determination to terminate the CYSS program can be seen in its actions in my own electorate this week. For some time representatives of the CYSS groups, CYSTEM, were able to attend the Charlestown Commonwealth Employment Service office to meet young unemployed people and display posters advising services offered by the group. A few days ago Pastor David Jones, its project officer, was advised to remove the posters and the group was evicted from the CES office. Pastor Jones, not to be deterred by the Government's ruthlessness, has set up a desk in the street near the CES office to carryon his work. This Government deserves the strongest condemnation for its abrupt abolition of the CYSS program and especially for its eviction of the CYSTEM group from the Charlestown CES premises.
However, the Government still has time to assist those young people and to redress the wrong. It can do that by reversing its decision to abolish the CYSS program and by giving an assurance that the program will continue.

Mr McLEAN (Perth) (9.39)-ln the context of this Budget. I believe that there is much evidence to suggest that the broad economic record of this Government has been one of considerable success when measured in terms of national indicators. Certainly, problems remain but the national performance to dflte has been creditable. The 1980-81 annual report of the Reserve Bank of Australia, a highly professional, objective and non-political body, stated:
Activity rose strongly in the Australian economy during 1980-81. This contrasted with generally depressed economic conditions overseas. Following healthy increases in 1979-80, real non-farm product and total employment recorded their highest rates of growth for more than half a decade. The recovery was broadly-based with strong contributions from spending on both capital formation and consumption.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1433

That comment came from the central bank of Australia. If we look at individual national economic indicators we see the same degree of success and confidence in the economy. The consumer price index increased by 8.8 per cent over the year to 30 June 1981-the best inflation result for years. Average weekly earnings rose by 14.1 per cent in 1980-81. When taken in conjunction with the inflation rate, this represents the largest increase in real wages for six years. Unemployment rose by 2.3 per cent during 1980-81. Youth unemployment, although still too high, fell by 17.6 per cent in the year to August 1981, while overall there was an increase in the number of employed people of 321,300 over the two years to July 1981. Real private business fixed investment expenditure grew by 22.5 per cent in real terms during 1980 and longer term investment anticipations data point to a continuation in 1981-82 of very
strong growth in business fixed investment.
This is all official data which indicates that, on a national basis, the Government has made substantial economic progress. I think it augers well for the longer term future of the Australian economy. It shows that the strategy of the Government in fighting inflation and in providing an atmosphere of confidence for consumers and investors is, despite the Government's critics, working well in a broad sense. The policy, as suggested by the indicators to which I have referred already, is providing the basis for strong, long term growth in the economy. But this does not overcome the fact that at present a large section of the community is being disadvantaged in the short term and in a very serious sense. I will refer to these groups, particularly home owners, a little later. Much of this problem is due not to the long term economic objectives of the Government but to the short term strategies adopted to meet those objectives. In this sense, the present Budget, although it has much to commend it, is an example.
The broad objective of the Budget is admirable but the strategy designed to achieve that objective is, I think, questionable. For example, the action taken to eradicate almost totally the Budget deficit is sound. After all, successive deficits and the need to borrow to finance them are a major cause of high interest rates. This Budget, by seeking almost to remove the deficit, is a step in the right direction towards easing upward pressures on interest rates. Furthermore, successive deficits and the need to finance them as they accumulate year on year simply mean that more and more taxpayers' dollars are used to finance previous government excesses rather than to finance present day social needs or to allow present day tax payers to retain more of their income through

tax reductions. So I think most reasonable people would agree that governments should live within their means. After all, it is not government money we are dealing with; it is the money of our constituents. To reduce or to eradicate the deficit is therefore a sound economic objective provided it does not hurt the needy and provided it does not create massive disincentives for people to earn or to spend their incomes and hence reduce their capacity to create additional wealth, productivity and employment for others.
I think we have achieved the desired reduction in the deficit by the wrong process. The overall Budget deficit is estimated to be only $146m for 1981-82. This is a great improvement on the deficit of $3,585m that this Government inherited in 1975-76 and the deficit levels which have prevailed since the early 1970s. But this has been achieved by increasing revenue rather than by reducing expenditure. In this context, the Budget strategy is almost classically anti-liberal. In the present Budget, for example, the Treasurer (Mr Howard) stated that in 1981-82 total outlays will increase by 12.6 per cent while total receiptsthat is, taxes of one kind or another-will increase by 15.8 per cent. In defence of the Government, one could say that it has kept the rate of growth in government expenditure and receipts at a much lower level than would the Labor Party if it were in office. The Labor Party is the socialist party in this country and true to its philosophy it constantly advocates policies which involve increased government expenditure, increased taxes and a reduced private sector. It is consistent for the Labor Party to argue along these lines, no matter how disastrous the consequences would be for the country.
But it is not consistent for this Government simply to reduce the rate of growth of the government sector. We have to reduce its size in absolute terms, and this is not being done. At present the Government is locked into a philosophically inconsistent position which is neither permitting the economy to achieve its full potential nor permitting the achievement of social equity. Of course the Labor Party seldom sees any connection between economic progress and the capacity to provide for the genuine welfare needs of the nation. But so long as this Government is determined, quite rightly, to reduce the deficit, but not to reduce government expenditure, it will remain locked into the classical anti-liberal position of constantly increasing taxes and reducing the size of the private sector. Therefore, at a time when demographic trends will be increasing the demands of an increasingly dependent section of the

1434 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

community, the capacity of the reduced productive sector of the economy-the private sector-will be falling. This eventually will place intolerable burdens on the economy and prevent rather then provide the attaining of both economic growth and social justice.
The decision on family allowances in the Budget reflects the kind of problem to which I refer. The increased family allowances are welcomed by all but the Government's decision to increase family allowances for all families with three or more children, irrespective of need, highlights the problem to which I have referred. The provision of government services to all, irrespective of need, means that the poor two children families in my electorate get nothing-except higher taxes. But in fact they should get the family allowance accruing to my family of almost five children because, frankly, I receive a good salary and do not need that kind of assistance.
If this kind of universal government expenditure persists and government expenditure is not directed as a top priority to those most in need, a number of things will happen, and we are seeing the beginning of this now. If this kind of universal tendency by governments to be popular by giving something to everybody irrespective of need does continue, we will see ever-increasing government expenditure. We will see ever-increasing government taxes. We will see the private sector fall away. The poor will not receive the priority help they need and there will be social inequity which in the long term will only be worsened if the basis of the Australian economy, the private sector, is not permitted to work to its full capacity.
With welfare benefits going to the rich as well as to the poor, with tax avoidance favouring the relatively well off notwithstanding the attempts of the Government to minimise this avoidance, with taxes on wageand salary earners rising to meet ever-increasing government expenditure and with interest rates responding to excessive government expenditure, the burden ultimately will be borne by the middle income wage and salary earners in our society. I only wish that the Labor Party, when it advocates ever-increasing levels of government expenditure and taxation-much higher than this Government would ever tolerate-would consider this fact. By not exercising Commonwealth expenditure restraint on the scale required, we move to higher tax impositions to meet our required deficit positions and we lose credibility with the States whom we ask to share this burden and who, in turn, impose taxes which affect all householders. Further, inevitably the financial demands of government also lead to

higher interest rates which impact on the same people.
On the question of interest rates, I simply want to remind the Government of the way in which interest rates affect the existing, let alone the prospective, home owner. I am not saying that the increase in interest rates was avoidable. Given our recent economic history it almost was impossible not to increase interest rates. I agree that when governments follow an expansive budgetary policy for any length of time, monetary policy inevitably has to bear the burden, and this is now the case through higher interest rates. Without debating the pros and cons of the economic decision itself, I simply remind the Government of the difficulties which these high and rising interest rates cause to so many home owners. I remember not so many years ago, before I became a member of this Parliament in the early 1970s when Labor was in government, how I personally felt the impact of very high interest rates. If I remember correctly, at that time. I was on a salary of about $8,000 per annum which in those days was in the typical middle to lower income bracket. I had a wife, three children, a mortgage and all the normal family expenses of health, education and so forth.
Dr Theophanous-You should have voted Labor.
Mr McLEAN-The Labor Party created those circumstances. I felt a very severe financial impact from the then historically high interest rates which occurred under the Labor Government. The financial strain on my family was enormous. We had to pull in our belts every way, delay paying important bills until well after their due date, take additional employment, and make responses such as that which, in a sense, I suppose are quite normal. But I make the point to the Government that high interest rates impose very great burdens, particularly on the low income and single income two-parent families. I repeat: At that stage I was on a middle.income level and not on a lower income level.
The monthly mortgage repayment is usually the largest bill a family has to pay, and the family has very little power or; discretion to change it. I make this point on behalf of many of the constituents I represent in the Parliament. These interest . rates constitute a further burden to the family unit, which already is hit by increasing State charges throughout Australia and by the indirect taxes imposed in this latest Budget. These comments lead me to two suggestions I want to make to the Government. The first concerns the need for the Government to undertake the preparation

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1435

of family impact statements. I have advocated this in the past, and I do so again. I am not calling for a national inquiry, an interdepartmental report or
any mechanism which is likely to produce results only after a lengthy, exhaustive examination. I believe that a small sample of families in each State with an average family income, with average family commitments of a mortgage, health insurance, education and the like, which one would attach to a family of, say, two or three children, should be surveyed. The inquiry should be official. It need not be based on a large sample, but it should involve representatives of each level of government in each State and Territory. It would involve collating data which I think already exists for other purposes. I say this because many people and organisations are making statements about the welfare of the family unit. People such as academics
and politicians and organisations such as political parties, churches, charitable and other nongovernment organisations all have been involved in making certain assertions about the relative well-being of family units.
I believe that, for the sake of objectivity, accuracy and credibility, an official study should be
undertaken in order that policy makers can proceed to make decisions on the basis of known facts. I do not criticise the work which has been done by many people in this area, but the results of studies to date and the assertions made by people and organisations involved in this issue seem to vary widely. We all-have our own views on this matter, no doubt coloured by our own prejudices. But no one seems to have a definitive answer, and we must have one if sensible official policy making is to be based on a concern for the family unit, which I regard as the fundamental base unit of any decent society.
Much comment has been made in my State about what constitutes an appropriate mix of revenue options. That deba.te has also been taking place at a Commonwealth level. But the fact remains that enormous changes have been taking place in many areas involving the familychanges to the health scheme, the taxation system, social security programs and education funding, high interest rates, changing roles of local government activities and so forth. At each level of government, departments take money away from the individual and the fl\mily unit, and other departments reallocate funds, either directly or indirectly, to other individuals and family units. I think it is time we knew exactly what the present position is in each State with regard to the average family unit and how that unit has fared in recent years. If we do not have that official information how can we sensibly develop policies to cater for

the family? I refer to policies regarding government expenditure, taxation relief and the mix of government revenue.
The other comment or suggestion I make to the Government, and it is related to the need for family impact statements to be developed, relates to the need, in my opinion, for future tax reductions
to be directed to the family unit. In a sense, I suppose, I am pre-judging the results of a family impact statement. We certainly will need to know the results of such a study before we know to what extent and in what form such assistance needs to be directed. But I am convinced that under the pressure of rising State charges in all States, rising interest rates and increased taxes-both direct and indirectthe average family unit is being put under increasing pressure. In the long term that is disastrous for any nation. I therefore say to the Government that when it starts to make plans for
its next Budget, tax relief of a very substantial nature must be directed to the 'family unit as a top priority over other tax relief measures. But as I said earlier, the extent and direction of such relief. should be based on official data of a kind which could come from a proper survey of the family unit,' conducted State by State and involving all three levels of government. If such relief is accompanied by sensible long term reductions in low priority government expenditure, lower taxation will be sustainable in the long term to the benefit of all. Government expenditure can then be confined to the genuinely needy, thereby providing the kind of equity which any rapidly growing economy can both morally and economically justify.
There are many aspects of the Budget which I commend. They include the increased funding of manpower and training schemes to assist the unemployed; the increased family allowances, although I believe these increases were implemented in the wrong way; the increased supplementary rental assistance for pensioners; the increased assistance for the handicapped; and, of course, the substantial reduction in the deficit, despite the way in which that reduction was achieved. In my opinion, the overall objective of the Budget is sound, but I have reservations about the way in which the Budget strategy has been designed to meet that objective. There is no doubt in my mind that the policies of the Government, since it came to office in 1975, have restored the basic strength of the Australian economy. In many senses we have led the world with our economic performance and have provided a sound economic base on which to make further solid progress throughout the 1980s. But if we are to maintain this momentum we need to look again at
