Appropriation Bill (No. I)

our economic strategy and ensure that by directing expenditure to the needy and by providing sustainable and substantial tax relief in the next Budget the economic promise we now have can be realised for the benefit of all Australians.
I express concern that it seems to be coming almost impossible for any government in this country to offer tax reductions to the Australian public. The public seems to have become used to the fact that its elected representatives can spend their money on their behalf much more efficiently than they can. When people are asked in isolation whether they want tax cuts, they say yes. When asked whether they want specific expenditure increases they say yes. When specific interest groups are asked whether they will accept specific expenditure cuts they respond by saying no. During the razor gang exercise, which involved some 350-odd decisions, about 350-odd specific interest groups came forward with good reasons why no action should have been taken to reduce Budget expenditure. At all times all people seem to want both increased expenditure and reduced taxes. The public does not seem to be able to accept the economic discipline which is required of government to do one or the other and to establish priorities. Examples of this are to be found in the Community Youth Support Scheme, the education debate in my State and the decisions of the razor gang. I believe it is becoming almost impossible, within our political system, to be able to withstand the political pressures which inevitably come when a government seeks to reduce government expenditure in order to offer lower taxes to the wider Australian community. We also face the same kind of pressures when we seek to reallocate expenditure from those with lower priority needs to those in genuine need. Nevertheless, I believe that the Government, which has been elected with a mandate to move towards lower taxation, smaller government and directing resources to those genuinely in need, should be resolute enough to proceed with that program notwithstanding what, in the short term might be politically disadvantageous flack, but which in the long term will be to the benefit to all.
Mrs DARLING (Lilley) (9.59)-There is one thing a Fraser-Howard Budget can always be relied on to do; that is to find some new and devious way of hitting the weakest in the community. This Budget plumbs new depths. The relative complacency of the Australian media following the Budget announcement indicates just how remarkably immune we all have become to tough Budgets. Over the last six years, and particularly in the four years the Treasurer (Mr Howard) has been on the job, the Government has succeeded in

anaesthetising the community-not that the poor can have the pain killed for them, considering the manner in which they have been treated. The Government has achieved this with a constant dose of fiscal restraint, belt tightening, union bludgeoning and a mixture of monetarist medicines; and all of its cures are worse than the disease.
Debate interrupted.

Dr BLEWETT (Bonython) (1O.0)-ln the adjournment debate last night the honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume), when talking of some remarks that I had supposedly made on the new health scheme, alleged that I had spread a particularly nonsensical proposition and that I was spreading untrue propaganda knowing it to be untrue. One would have thought, if remarks like that were made, that some evidence would have been provided to sustain them. Not a single jot of evidence was provided by the honourable member for Macarthur to suggest that I had ever made the alleged remarks. It was simply slander without substance and smear without argument, and I think one can leave it there and take no further notice of it.
I take the opportunity tonight to talk about misleading propaganda in the health field. Unlike the honourable member for Macarthur, I ,will sustain the argument about misleading propaganda by this Government in relation to the latest health scheme. I refer to a pamphlet published by the Department of Health and called 'In sickness and in health-better value for your dollar'. The phrase 'better value for your dollar' is a pretty subjective statement about the new health scheme and has aroused considerable debate and doubt. It is certainly a subjective rather than an objective description of the new health scheme. In that pamphlet one whole page was devoted simply to propagandising the scheme-not giving people information about the health scheme so that they could make their decisions but simply propagandising the scheme. The Doctors Reform Society attacked that pamphlet as being misleading and not helping people to make accurate decisions. It was particularly misleading about the hospital only insurance option. The Australian Consumers

Adjournment

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1437

Association attacked the pamphlet. Two governments, the Liberal Government of South Australia and the Labor Government in New South Wales, found it necessary to issue corrective pamphlets so that people could have accurate and reliable information on which to make decisions.
We now learn that even the Government has admitted that it was a misleading pamphlet because it has printed a new version, a sanitised version, of the health pamphlet. It has removed from the cover any of the subjective statements. The pamphlet is simply called 'Hospital and medical cover from I September'. There is no longer on the cover anything about 'better value for your dollar'. Perhaps the Government is now admitting . that there is no 'better value for your dollar' in the scheme. That has certainly gone from the front of the pamphlet. The whole panel devoted to propagandising the scheme has been eliminatedthere is no sign of it at all-and the hospital only criticism has been removed entirely from the pamphlet. The new pamphlet was obviously a rush job. I notice that it was put out by Hot Type Phototypesetters. Some four weeks after the Government spent $160,000 on this misleading pamphlet, it has now spent another $30,000 of taxpayers' money putting out this revamped, sanitised version.
The Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar), when he tries to explain it, says: 'We have produced this new pamphlet for public hospital people'. What on earth has removing the phrase 'better value for your dollar' got to do with public hospital patients? What on earth has the whole panel devoted to propagandising the scheme got to do with public hospital patients? That is simply hogwash from the Minister to explain the publication of the new pamphlet. It is quite clear that the new pamphlet was produced because the Government implicitly admits that its first pamphlet was misleading and inaccurate and it found it necessary to put out not a propaganda document but an objective account of the new health scheme. As I said, the explanations of the Minister do not wash. Unfortunately for the taxpayers of this country it has cost them an extra $30,000 for this revamped, sanitised and non-propagandistic version of the Government's new health scheme. It has cost the people that money.
We do not know yet how many of the original pamphlets were simply pulped when the Government made its decision to produce the new and sanitised version. I suggest that if people make allegations about the new health scheme they bring evidence to bear. In conclusion I make the point again to the honourable member for Macarthur

that if he wants to make allegations about what I

say, let us have the real evidence. I say that the

Government, by this new production, has im-

plicitly admitted the misleading nature of its orig-

inal health pamphlet, and the evidence is there for

all to see.

Mr DONALD CAMERON (Fadden) (10.5)-1 was present at the hearing of Estimates Committee B the other night when the matters spoken of by the previous speaker, the honourable member for Bonython (Dr Blewett), were raised in the presence of the Minister for Health (Mr MacKellar). I am sure that if honourable members refer to the Hansard record of that Committee's proceedings last Thursday night they will receive all the answers to the comments of the honourable member for Bonython.

My electorate of Fadden takes in the town of Canungra and a large rural area outside that town. However, the land warfare camp in Canungra is in the Federal electorate of McPherson. The problem is that'in the Army camp there is growing a noxious bush called groundsel. Once or twice a year the seeds of this plant are blown out of the Army camp, which is Commonwealth property, into my electorate. The laws of the State require local authorities to stamp upon any private individual having groundsel growing on his or her property. Unfortunately the problems concerning herbicides in recent years have caused the Commonwealth Government or the Department of Defence to suspend temporarily the use of sprays whilst inquiries and investigations are carried out. In the meantime the Department of Defence and the people who sit in the departments here in Canberra seem to have the view that groundsel is just a bush that grows and that, if a few soldiers with machetes go in and cut it down, that is the way to eliminate and eradicate it. I want to show the House a piece off a small branch of a groundsel tree. I must admit that I have brought this all the way from the electorate of McPherson to show the House and the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh). I wish the Minister for Defence (Mr Killen) was in the chamber to see that groundsel is simply not a little plant that one can take to with a machete or one of those spray cans of weed killer.

Mr Humphreys-How old is that tree?

Mr DONALD CAMERON-I have been trying to count the rings but I am not familiar enough with groundsel to know the number of years. The point I am trying to make is that I have had to go to this extreme length after three or four years of fighting a bitter campaign for the removal of the groundsel. What happens is that the seeds fly into

1438 REPRESENTATIVES 16 September 1981

Adjournment

my electorate, the plants grow and the council gets on to my constituents who get onto me and say: 'The seeding is coming out of Commonwealth ground; what are you going to do about it'? It has been a thousand-day fight. I have got nowhere. I have turned to the only area where I believe there may be help. From the interest that has been displayed here tonight, I feel our luck may be changing and that people may well take notice that groundsel is not simply a little green bush that can be treated lightly but that it can grow into a large tree which a man cannot get his hands around. Mr Deputy Speaker, I see you shaking your head in acknowledgement that both my hands do not get around this part of the groundsel tree. In future I require the Department not to regard it as a bush, because if it is not treated it will become as large as this and will need more than spray cans and soldiers with machetes to control it. I ask you, Mr Deputy Speaker, as the custodian of this House, to ensure that the Minister for Defence and the Minister for Housing and Construction are acquainted with my plea first thing tomorrow. I seek your advice as to where I should put this bit of timber.
MrFife-Perhaps the honourable member would like to table the exhibit.
Mr DONALD CAMERON-Mr Deputy Speaker, I would like to table it.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)-I think the honourable member might best serve the House by marking the log clearly so that it cannot be confused with another member.
Mr HOLDING (Melbourne Ports) (10.10)-1 was a member of the Estimates Committee which dealt with the estimates of the Department of the . Prime Minister and Cabinet. I noted with interest that in terms of the Budget allocation for the library and facilities of the Department there was a slight increase. We were looking at an estimate for that Department of some $70,000-odd for books and periodicals. I did not have any objection to that and neither did any other member of the committee. Of course, the Department and advisers ought to have appropriate library facilities because knowledge is very important and we would not want that Department to be deprived of it. However, there seems to me to be a double set of standards.
At the same time that the Prime Minister's Department has no difficulty in maintaining the level of Budget estimates for the provision of its library facilities the Library of this Parliament suffers an overall reduction of 20 per cent without any discussion or reference to the Parliament, and indeed from what we know, without any discussion or

reference to the Presiding Officers of this Parliament. I, as a member of this Parliament who believes in the importance of the independence of the parliamentary institution, want to place my protest on record. I believe the people of Australia view this Parliament in terms .of the Westminster tradition. They see us as beingwhatever our imperfections-the custodian and protector of the parliamentary rights which have been created by the Constitution. Under the Westminster system the concept is that the Executive is answerable to the Parliament for its actions. But we are seeing in Australia the slow and continual erosion of the rights of the parliamentary institution so that the real struggle for political power, and the exercise of that power in Australia, is no longer a struggle between the government of the day subject to the sanctions and the mandates of the Parliament; it has become increasingly a struggle between the Executive, the ministry and the bureaucracy, with members of the Parliament for the most part interested by-standers trying to get in wherever they can to express a point of view.
I believe it is time for all members of this Parliament, across party political lines, to be prepared to stand up to say: 'We have a duty to this institution to make it work more successfully and more appropriately than it is working at the moment'. I have had the benefit-as have some other honourable members-of serving in more than one parliament. When I came to the national Parliament I believed, like most other people, that because it was the national Parliament, in terms of the Westminster tradition, it would be a true custodian of that tradition as compared with the State parliamentary institution in which I served. I am bound to inform the House that from my experience, observations and involvement that is not the case. Parliamentarians in the national Parliament have far fewer rights than parliamentarians in the State Parliament in which I served. I believe that is true generally. Parliamentarians and this institution must be prepared to protect and preserve the prerogatives of this institution. We do not mind if Prime Ministers want to have Budget allocations which are appropriate because we think they should have the best information possible. But just as that standard is good enough for the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and his Department it has to be good enough .for the Parliament. To cut the Parliamentary Library facilities by 20 per cent is to cut the flow of knowledge to every honourable member of this Parliament and to diminish the capacity of every parliamentarian to fulfil his obligations to this institution.

Petrol Prices
(Question No. 2302)
(Replaces previous answer, Hansard page 3594, 10 June 1981)
Mr Jacobi asked the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs, upon notice, on 14 May 1981.
(I) Has his allention been drawn to the fact that since the release of the recent Prices Justification Tribunal (PJT) report on petrol prices, the extent of discounting petrol has increased markedly in New South Wales, Victoria and Queensland.
(2) Is it a fact that South Australian motorists have not shared in the benefits of the resultant lower level of petrol prices.
(3) Are South Australians now paying the highest petrol prices in Australia.
(4) Is he satisfied that petrol resellers and motorists in South Australia are not being discriminated against by the oil companies: if not, will he (a) instruct the body replacing the PJT to investigate immediately this discrimination buy the oil companies in South Australia and (b) continue to monitor petrol pricing throughout Australia.
Mr Moore-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No. Indeed I have noted reports that some oil companies intend to withdraw discounts, following the release of the PJT report of 31 March 1981 on Petroleum product prices. I am not able to confirm or deny the accuracy of those reports nor to say whether market conditions will enable companies to withdraw those discounts.
(2) See answer to (I) above.
(3) According to ABS figures (Catalogue No. 6403.0) the average retail price of super grade petrol in Adelaide during the March quarter of 1981 was higher than in other State capitals. In the previous quarter the reverse applied.
(4) Prices determined by the PJT for the oil companies are maximum justified prices. They differ between States only to the extend of differences in State taxes or other charges levied on petroleum products.
Oil companies are not obliged to charge the maximum justified prices and, where competition is keen, they might not be able to obtain the full justified price. Actual prices to resellers would reflect both the strength of competition in particular localities and the operation of any State price controls that may apply.
The Petroleum Products Pricing Authority will take over from the PJT the responsibility for monitoring prices charged by the oil companies. It will have powers as proposed in the Petroleum Products Pricing Bill which I introduced into Parliament on 27 May 1981.
As the Commonwealth's jurisdiction over prices is limited to the corporate sector, effective action to monitor retail petrol prices, including the margins applied by non-corporate resellers, is a maller for State Governments to consider.

Brokerage Houses
(Question No. 2207)
Mr Jacobi asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 12 May 1981:
(I) Can he provide an estimate of the sum lost in terms of life assurance or superannuation premiums and the loss involved in terms of sums assured, as a consequence of the large number of brokerage house collapses.
(2) Will he also provide statistics in relation to the items referred to in part (I) for (a) permanent assurance, (b) temporary assurance and (c) superannuation cover.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) There are no official statistics available 011 the amount of life insurance business transacted through insurance brokers; however, I understand that the proportion of life insurance business so transacted is small. For this reason and because life insurance cover is not usually granted until a life insurance company receives the premium it would appear that the amount of losses of the kind referred to by the honourable member would also be small.
Hospitals Contribution Fund of Australia: Travel Industry
(Question No. 2425)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) Is he able to state whether HCF proposes to expand into the travel industry.
(2) If so, is this (a) a legitimate avenue of activity for a fund whose main role will soon be as agent for the Commonwealth in the payment of medical and hospital benefits and (b) analagous to that fund's earlier purchase of executive planes at the expense of fund contributors.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) To my knowledge, HCF proposes to enter into .the travel agency business. This appears evident from the fact that the organisation has established a subsidiary company-HCF Travel Pty Ltd.
However, I understand that HCF has not formally made application to the State authorities for registration as a travel agent. Apparently, there are a number of formalities that need to be completed before making such application for registration, e.g. recognition within the international travel industry.
(2) (a) In addition to being registered under the National Health Act as a medical and hospital benefits organisation, HCF is also registered as a company (limited by guarantee) under the New South Wales Companies Act. As such, HCF is empowered under its Memorandum and Articles of Association to carry out activities that may seem to HCF to be capable of being conveniently carried out in connection with its health insurance activities or calculated to directly or indirectly enhance the value or render profitable any of the company's property or rights.
(b) I see no analogy between HCF entering the travel business and its purchase some years ago of an aeroplane. The cost of the aeroplane was a charge against the organisation's management expenses.
The establishment by HCF of a subsidiary company to undertake travel agency business could only be viewed as an investment activity. The National Health Act governs the extent to which moneys can be used by registered health

benefits organisations to pay benefits and to meet costs of carrying on business as registered organisations. However, the Act does not specifically control the nature of investments of moneys held by such organisations. Investments by registered health benefits organisations are generally matters for the individual organisations to determine. I would be concerned, however, to ensure that available moneys are invested reasonably, to the advantage of contributors, and there are other powers available to me under the Act to ensure this is done.
It is not unusual for registered health benefits organisations to engage in other activities. Some registered organisations, including HCF, conduct general insurance through subsidiary companies.
Family Medicine Program
(Question No. 2435)
Mr Hayden asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to a report entitled 'Funding Conflicts in Times of Constraint' in the National News of the Family Medicine Program of May/June 1981.
(2) If so, has he noted in that report the statement that the Family Medicine Program is having difficulty training existing and increased numbers of general practitioners under the program, and the question posed which asked can the country not afford to train these doctors as they will be controlling health care costs for the next 40 or 50 years.
(3) Is there any substance in this statement; if so, given the Government's concern with cost containment, what will the Government do to educate medical practitioners in costeffectiveness within their practices.
(4) Is it a fact that the current avenue of this education, the Family Medicine Program, has had no increase in funds since 1979.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) Yes.
(3) General practitioners generate health costs - in the course of their own practices, by referral of patients to specialists, and by arranging admission of patients to hospitals and nursing homes. It can therefore be said that general practitioners have.a significant influence on health costs.
Although the Government is conc~rned with the contain-
ment of health costs, the Commonwealth does not have sole responsibility for promoting cost containment; the States and the medical profession itself also have obligations to the community to contain costs as far as practicable.
The Government has decided to institute an independent review of policy issues surrounding the post-graduate vocational training activities undertaken by the Family Medicine Program. Having regard to the outcome of that review, the Government will decide on what future actions are warranted on its part in relation to post-graduate vocational training of general practitioners, including training related to costeffectiveness within their practices.
(4) The allocations in respect of the Family Medicine Program (including a small component related to Australian Medical Students' Association) since 1978-79 have been:
$m .1978-79-4.478 1979-80-4.676 1980-81-5.107 1981-82-5.025

Trunk Calls Booking Fee
(Question No. 2609)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) Did Telecom Australia, during the dispute of May/June 1981, continue to Follow its normal practice of charging a fee for booked trunk calls which could have been made by Subscriber Trunk Dialling (STD); if so, who was responsible for this decision.
(2) What was the financial return to Telecom as a result of adopting this practice, during the period of the industrial dispute referred to in part (I).
Mr Sinclair-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) I am advised by Telecom that an operator surcharge is applied on all trunk calls booked with an exchange operator which could have been made with STD. However, an exchange manager may waive application of the operator surcharge, when in his opinion, calls to STD destinations are made via the manual service because of some local breakdown 'or difficulty affecting the STD service. In accordance with this procedure, the surcharge was waived at various times at many centres during the period referred to by the honourable member. Unfortunately, statistics of the nature sought are not available.
Pharmaceutical Benefits Scheme Schedule
(Question No. 2680)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 18 August 1981:
(I) How many items have been (a) dropped from and (b) added to the Pharmaceutical Benefits Scheme Schedule since 1975.
(2) What has been the (a) saving and (b) cost to his Department of each deletion and addition.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) 437 ready-prepared items and 109 drugs for use in preparations compounded by the pharmacist have been removed.
(b) 284 ready-prepared items have been added.
(2) It is not possible to determine the degree of transfer of prescribing from or to other items as a result of additions to or deletions from the list of pharmaceutical benefits. Accordingly it is not possible to assess the net savings or cost of each of these changes to the list. Items were added to the list on the recommendation of the Pharmaceutical Benefits Advisory Committee (PBAC) which is established under the National Health Act to give advice on medical grounds on matters concerning the pharmaceutical benefits list. The deletions from the list comprise items deleted on the advice of the PBAC and items discontinued by the manufacturer.

Orthomolecular Medicine Concepts
(Question No. 2753)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 19 Aug!lst 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to evidence supporting orthomolecular medicine concepts in (a) Biological Basis of Schizophrenia by Hemmings, MTP Press Limited, (b) Senate Select Hearings, 1980 Update, Library of Congress 79-57366: (c) Reply to the Task Force Report 1976 by Hoffer et al; (d) Dr C. M. Reading's address to the Government Schedule Revision Committee, Journal of Orthomolecular Psychiatry, Biomedical Library, University of N.S.W. and (e) a note in Index Medicus, March 1980.
(2) Has the Government a report on the need for objective evaluation of costs and benefits of orthomolecular medical practice; if so, is it available to Members ofParliament.

Answers to Questions

16 September 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 1449

Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) I rely for advice on professional matters relating to particular medical practices, such as orthomolecular medicine, on the people best suited to provide it. In the case of orthomolecular medicine I sought counsel on its effectiveness from the Royal Australian and New Zealand College of Psychiatrists, the Royal Australian College of Physicians and the Royal College of Pathologists of Australasia and subsequently from the Medical Benefits Schedule Revision Committee. My reason for doing so was that practitioners of orthomolecular medicine typically order a large number of pathology tests for their patients in order to discover evidence of excessive or deficient intake of heavy metals or vitamins, allergic conditions or manifestations of autoimmune disease. Unfortunately this is done at heavy cost to the medical benefits system. '[he advice received from the above mentioned professional bodies was that orthomolecular medicine has no status in the practice of medicine or psychiatry, its clinical role is unproven and the pathology tests cannot be justified for the rational practice of internal medicine or psychiatry.
In the circumstances I took the step of declaring such pathology tests to be health screening and therefore in breach of section 19 (5) of the Health Insurance Act, which prevents payment of Commonwealth Medical Benefits for health screening services (i.e. tests not reasonably required for management of the medical condition of the patient). I have no reason to believe that the above professional bodies did other than carry out a proper investigation, including reference to appropriate literature on orthomolecular medicine.
(2) The Government has not received a 'report on the need for objective evaluation of costs and benefits of orthomolecular medical practice'. The Government is fully aware of the need for objective evidence of the effectiveness of orthomolecular medicine and is prepared to accept such evidence and act on it should it be forthcoming. I have notified this view to various supporters and practitioners of orthomolecular medicine who have made representations to me on the subject.
Queensland Goverpment Aircraft: Priority in Landing
(Question No. 2780)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:
(I) Has the pilot of the Queensland Government plane used by the Queensland Premier ever requested permission to jump the queue of aircraft in a holding pattern awaiting landingat (a) Brisbane or (b) Sydney Airport.
(2) If so, what were the reasons for the request.
(3) Was permission given; ifso, for what reasons.
(4) Does queue jumping disadvantage and delay other aircraft, particularly civil aircraft.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable members question is as follows:
(I) I am advised that no direct requests from the aircraft used by the Queensland Premier to jump the queue of holding aircraft are contained on record at either Brisbane or Sydney. However, when known that the Premier was aboard, efforts have been made at Brisbane to expedite the movement of that aircraft, without upsetting the established movement priorities of other aircraft being sequenced at the time.
(2) Not applicable.
(3 j' See answer to ( I ) above.

(4) When delays to aircraft movements are being experienced, queue jumping, if permitted, would cause further delay to other aircraft of similar or higher priority. For this reason every effort is made to sequence aircraft movements in accordance with the laid down schedule of priorities.
United States Federal Aviation Administration Regulations
(Question No, 2785)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:
(1) Can he say what requirements the United States of America Federal Aviation Administration make in regard to the fitting of anti-pollution devices and apparatus to jet aircraft operating in that country.
(2) If so, are the requirements of the United States Federal Aviation Administration stronger than Government regulations in Australia.
Mr Hunt--;-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The United States Department of Transportation Federal Aviation Administration requirements for the control of air pollutants emitted by jet aircraft relate to prohibition of fuel venting and limits on the emission of smoke from the engines.
(2) The Australian requirements for air pollutant emission from jet aircraft and engines will be consistent with internationally adopted Standards established under the Chicago Convention on International Civil Aviation. These are to be published in Annex 16 (Volume 2) to that Convention and they are expected to come into effect on 30 October 1981. Formal regulations to implement them will be established in due course. In the meantime, implementation of the principles of the Standards have been encouraged by the Department of Transport and Australian airlines currently comply with them. It is understood that the United States and Australian regulations will be similar, if not the same.

Research into Endoparasites of Ruminants
(Question No. 2800)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 19 August 1981:
(I) Has the CSIRO been allocated $62,900 from the Meat Research Trust Fund for research in 1981-82 into endoparasites of ruminants in southern Australia.
(2) If so, in what institutions or locations will this research be carried out.
(3) What are the aims and objectives of the project.
(4) Who will be the personnel involved, or, how many persons will be involved.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) CSIRO have been allocated $49,000 For this project.
(2) At the Animal Health Research Laboratory, CSIRO Division of Animal Health, Parkville, Victoria, with field work being undertaken at Werribee and Caramut in Victoria.
(3) The objective and General Outline of AMRC Project CS9, Section A, which commenced on I July 1979 and is due for completion on 30 June 1982, are as follows:
(a) Objective: To define the factors controlling the presence of in-
testinal parasites and production loss Following infections of cattle and sheep, with the aim of establishing principles of optimum control at the lowest possible cost for increased productivity.
(b) General outline: The availability of infective larvae in pasture is the
key factor leading to parasitic gastroenteritis in sheep and cattle. In Western Victoria the availability of larvae depends mainly on the number of worm eggs deposited during autumn, and the subsequent .weather conditions. If the weather is favourable, high numbers of larvae occur on pastures during the cool winter and early spring months, but few survive during the hot, dry months of summer.
Consequently, control strategies being studied are based on: (a) reducing worm egg contamination of pastures during autumn,or (b) avoiding the use of pastures known to be heavily contaminated.
CSIRO will be making application to AMRC For funds to enable these evaluation experiments to continue over a number of years in order to take account of seasonal variability.
(4) The personnel involved are: Dr N. Anderson-Project Supervisor Mr P. J. Martin-Experimental Officer Mr I. K. McTaggart-Senior Technical Officer Mr M. A. Pope-Technical Officer Ms D. Williamson-Technical Assistant
International Code of Marketing of Breast Milk Substitutes
(Question No. 2806)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 20 August 1981:
(I) Did Australia support the International Code of Marketing of Breast Milk Substitutes at the 34th World Health Assembly.
(2) If so, when will he introduce legislation to (a) implement the recommendations of the Code in respect of the marketing of breast 'milk substitutes within Australia and (b)

require Australian companies and authorities operating outside Australia to conform to the recommendations of the Code.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes.
(2) (a) and (b) The Code has been reFerred by the World Health Orgapization to all Member States as a recommendation only-its implementation is not mandatory. Under these circumstances, it is expected that industry compliance with the Code will be on the basis of self-regulation. Indeed, in Australia negotiations are under way with relevant industry representatives to seek an undertaking from the industry that it will voluntarily comply with the provisions of the Code.
Specific implementation aspects of the Code-for example, labelling requirements-are presently being considered by relevant committees of the National Health and Medical Research Council.
The Code has been referred to all State and Territory health authorities for consideration as regards possible implementation action within their respective jurisdictions.
The Commonwealth Department of Health will be responsible for monitoring the implementation by industry of the principles and aims of the Code.
The Commonwealth does not intend-nor does the Constitution empower it-to introduce legislation on the matter.
Changing Needs of Pensioners
(Question No. 2809)
Mr Good luck asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 20 August 1981:
Would it be possible to form a Parliamentary Committee to look into the immediate and changing needs of the pensioners of Australia; if so, will he undertake to initiate this.
Mr Anthony-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
Given the comprehensive system of review which already operates to advise the Government on matters affecting pensioners, the Government does not propose to move to establish such a Parliamentary Committee.

Export Potential of Local Flower Market
(Question No. 2836)
Mr Les Johnson asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 26 August 1981:
(I) When will the Government release the report on the export potential of the local lIower market known as the ESAMS study.
(2) What has been the cost of the study.
Mr Thomson-The answers to the honourable member's questions are as follows:
(I) The study referred to was the first phase of a public interestproject being undertaken by my Department to assist the Australian nursery industry in penetrating the export markets for indoor foliage plants.
As well as achieving its major objective of identifying technology requirements, the study generally identified Western European and US markets totalling $A I,300m per year and analysed the Australian industry's capability to penetrate that market.
The results' of the study were presented at a seminar in December 1980. Because of public interest shown in the months following the seminar, it was decided to arrange for publication of the report.
To allow early industry comment on the study, a limited number of copies (33) of a draft report have been distributed to the nursery industry through the Australian Nurserymen's Association and its affiliated State Associations. Industry members wishing to preview the draft report should contact the Association in their State.
Presently this report is undergoing final editing prior to publication by the AGPS near the end of September.
(2) The cost of the study, including seminar and publication costs was $120,000.

Prescription Payments to Pharmacists
(Question No. 2896)
Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 27 August 1981:
(I) How many prescriptions are processed by his Department in each State and Territory.
(2) What is the value of these prescription claims in each State and Territory.
(3) Is the processing by his Department of prescription payments to pharmacists taking between 4-6 weeks in Queensland; ifso, what are the reasons for this delay.
(4) Has the workload of his Department in Queensland been augmented by additional processing work which has been transferred from New Sou~h Wales.

Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(3) My Department undertakes to pay pharmacists' claims for NHS and RPBS dispensing within thirty calendar days of
receipt. This target is currently being met in all Divisional Offices, including Queensland. The majority of NHS and RPBS claims processed for payment by the Queensland Div-
isional Office on my Department on 28 August 1981 were received on 7 and 4 August 1981, respectively.

(4) Processing of both NHS and RPBS claims from certain
pharmacists in north-east New South Wales has been transferred on a temporary basis to the Queensland Divisional Office of my Department. This transfer is one of a number of suc-
cessful measures undertaken to overcome a processing backlog which developed in New South Wales earlier this year.
Appropriate staffing adjustments have been made to compen-
sate for the increased workload in Queensland.

(5) As I stated in (3), my Department undertakes to pay
claims within thirty calendar days of receipt which is con-
sidered to be normal commercial practice. I am advised that suppliers may seek payment within a shorter period but this is not the normal or long-standing practice. The shorter period,
where it occurs, is normally associated with an incentive discount or bonus arrangement. Suppliers do not penalise phar-
macists who pay within thirty days.

Ansett Transport Industries: Capital Transactions
(Question No. 2898)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 27 August 1981:
Will he arrange for the capital transactions of Ansett Transport Industries, to the extent that they concern the 1981 Airlines Agreement, to be disclosed to the Parliament as completely as capital transactions of the Australian national Airlines Commission; if not, why not.
Mr Hunt-The anwer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
I am unaware of any capital transactions by either Ansett Transport Industries or the Australian National Airlines Commission which are directly concerned with the 1981 Airlines Agreement. I should note that clause 13 of the 1981 Airlines Agreement places virtually the same requirement to provide financial information to Parliament on Ansett Transport industries and the Australian National Airlines Commission.
The clause provides for both the Commission and Ansett Transport Industries to furnish separately to the Minister for Transport for presentation to the Parliament, as soon as practicable after the end of the financial year, financial information in respecl of their respective businesses and those of their subsidiary companies relating to:
(a) the operation of passenger air services on trunk routes, including such other passenger air services as are provided as ancillary to the services on trunk routes; and
(b) the total operation of air services carried on by the Commission and the Company.
The financial information provided by Ansett shall be generally in accordance with the accounting requirements of the Victorian Companies Act, be audited and certified as to correctness by two Directors of the Company.
The financial information provided by the Commission shall be audited by the Auditor-General and certified as to correctness by two Commissioners. When the Commission becomes a public company, the financial information shall be generally in accordance with the accounting requirements of the law relating to companies in the State or Territory of its incorporation, be audited in accordance with the provisions of that law and'certfied as to correctness by two Directors.
